The Stars of the Eighth Sphere and Their Magnitudes
The author describes the stars of the eighth sphere and their seven degrees of magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arrangement of stars in the eighth sphere according to their seven magnitudes and why this sphere is called al-Kursi (the Chair).

Core Idea
The eighth sphere's varying thickness gives it the name al-Kursi, with stars ranked in seven magnitudes.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah describes the celestial eighth sphere, known as the Chair. The author explains how the stars are arranged by size into seven ranks, and why the brightest star, Sirius, occupies the highest degree. This lesson unpacks that description and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's description of the eighth sphere and its stars come through.
Notice the term 'الكرسي' (the Chair) is introduced.
2
Identify the seven magnitudes
Find where the author lists the seven ranks of magnitude, from smallest (seventh) to largest (first). See how they correspond to degrees in the sphere.
3
Understand why the sphere is called al-Kursi
The author gives two reasons for the sphere's name: the varying degrees in its thickness and the seating of stars at different levels. Reflect on this.
The name 'Chair' suggests a structure with levels.
4
Consider the special status of Sirius
Note that Sirius (al-Shi'ra al-Yamaniya) is identified as the largest star, occupying the highest degree. Why is this logically necessary?
5
Reflect on the divine knowledge of stars' properties
The author concludes that each star has unique properties known only to Allah. Contemplate the limit of human knowledge emphasized here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكواكب المركوزة في الفلك الثامن: أجرام مختلفة بالصغر والكبر، والتي أدركتها أبصار الخلق من تلك الكواكب مرتبة على سبع مراتب في العظم، فأصغرها جرماً، ما يكون في العظم السابع، وهو يكون في أدنى الدرجات، وفوقها ما يكون في العظم السادس. وهكذا تترقى مرتبة فمرتبة، ودرجة فدرجة، إلى أن تصل إلى العظم الأول. ولهذا السبب حصل في ثخن الفلك الثامن: درجات متفاوتة. ولهذا سمي بالكرسي. ولما كانت الشعرى اليمانية، أكبرها قدراً، وجب أن يكون مكانه في الدرجة الأعلى، والمرتبة الأسمى والأسنى. وأقول: لا شك أن لكل واحد من تلك الكواكب خواص وآثار، لا يعلمها إلا الله سبحانه وتعالى.
The stars fixed in the eighth sphere are bodies differing in smallness and bigness, and those of them perceived by the sight of creatures are arranged in seven ranks of magnitude. Their smallest in body is that which is in the seventh magnitude, and it is in the lowest degree, and above it is that which is in the sixth magnitude, and so on ascending rank by rank and degree by degree until reaching the first magnitude. For this reason, there occur in the thickness of the eighth sphere varying degrees, and for this reason it is called the Chair (al-Kursi). And since Sirius al-Yamaniya is the greatest of them in magnitude, it is necessary that its place be in the highest degree and the most elevated and sublime rank. And I say: There is no doubt that each of those stars has properties and effects known only to Allah—may He be glorified and exalted.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a description of the celestial sphere known as the eighth sphere, which is the Chair (al-Kursi). The author explains that the stars embedded in this sphere have different sizes, and the visible stars are classified into seven magnitudes. The smallest stars are in the seventh magnitude (lowest degree), and they increase in size up to the first magnitude. The varying sizes cause the thickness of the eighth sphere to have different degrees, which is why it is called the Chair. The brightest star, Sirius (al-Shi'ra al-Yamaniya), is the largest and thus occupies the highest degree. The author concludes that each star has unique properties and effects known only to God.

Key Terms
الفلك الثامن (eighth sphere)
الكرسي (the Chair)
الشعرى اليمانية (Sirius)
سبع مراتب (seven ranks/degrees)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the seven magnitudes refer to the number of stars, but the author clarifies they are ranks of magnitude (size).
Correction: The seven magnitudes are classifications of size, not a count of stars. The passage describes stars arranged in seven ranks of magnitude, from seventh (smallest) to first (largest).

Checkpoint
What is the reason the eighth sphere is called the Chair (al-Kursi)?
A) Because it contains the greatest stars
B) Because of the varying thickness and degrees
C) Because it is the seat of God
D) Because it is the highest sphere

Key Takeaway
The eighth sphere is called al-Kursi because its thickness varies with the different magnitudes of stars, creating levels like a chair.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the stars' properties and effects are known only to Allah. In your daily life, where do you rely on human knowledge versus trusting Allah's exclusive knowledge? Are you willing to accept your limits before Him, or do you secretly wish to control what only He knows? Commit to one practical way this week to surrender that area to Allah.
?
The eighth sphere is called al-Kursi because of its ascending magnitudes, like steps leading higher. What are the 'steps' in your own life that you are using to ascend toward Allah? Are they worldly achievements or sincere worship? Pause and honestly examine if your ladder is built on humility or pride.
?
Sirius is the greatest star in the eighth sphere, yet the author ends by saying only Allah knows its true effects. Have you ever glorified a creation—a person, a status, a talent—forgetting that its ultimate reality is hidden in Allah’s knowledge? How can you redirect that admiration into recognition of the Creator alone? Make a firm intention to practice this redirection starting today.

Intellects, Souls, and the Eight Bearers of the Throne
The author presents the philosophical view of each sphere's intellect, soul, and the eight bearers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical interpretation of each celestial sphere having an intellect, a soul, and six directional spirits, and how this totals eight bearers of the Throne as mentioned in the Quran.

Core Idea
Each sphere has eight spiritual components: intellect, soul, and six directional spirits.

In this investigation, the author presents a philosophical model of the celestial spheres that aligns with Quranic verses. He explains that the philosophers posit each sphere has an intellect and a soul, and further divides into six directions, each with a governing spirit, totaling eight. This is then connected to the eight bearers of the Throne in Surah Al-Haqqah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the author's presentation. Note the structure: first the intellect and soul, then the six directions, then the total of eight, and finally the Quranic references.
Pay attention to phrases like 'لا يبعد' and 'الله أعلم' that indicate the tentative nature of the interpretation.
2
Identify the components of each sphere
The author identifies an intellect, a soul, and six directional spirits for each sphere. Count them: intellect (1) + soul (1) + six spirits (6) = eight. This is the total the author focuses on.
The six directions are right, left, front, back, above, below.
3
Understand the Quranic connection
The author points to Quran 69:17 about eight bearers of the Throne and then to 39:75 about angels surrounding the Throne. He sees these as references to the philosophical model.
The author is not claiming this is the only interpretation, but that it is plausible.
4
Reflect on the author's attitude
Notice that the author uses 'لا يبعد' (it is not far) and 'الله أعلم' (Allah knows best). This shows he is presenting a plausible philosophical view, not a dogmatic truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ههنا مباحث: المبحث الأول: إن الحكماء بينوا: أن لكل فلك عقلاً ونفساً. وبينوا أيضاً: أن كل فلك فإنه ينقسم أيضاً بحسب الجهات الست إلى أقسام سنة. فللفلك يمين وشمال، وقدام وخلف، وفوق وتحت. فلا يبعد أن يحصل له بحسب كل قسم من هذه الأقسام الستة: روح يدبره. والفلاسفة أثبتوا المجموع كل فلك عقلاً ونفساً فيكون المجموع: ثمانية. وإليه الإشارة بقوله تعالى: (ويحمل عرش ربك فوقهم يومئذ ثمانية) ثم لا يبعد أيضاً: أن يتولد عن كل واحد من تلك الأرواح القوية القاهرة: شعب ونتائج. الله أعلم بعددها. وإليه الإشارة بقوله تعالى: (وترى الملائكة حافين من حول العرش، يسبحون بحمد ربهم) الآية.
Then here are investigations: The first investigation: The philosophers have explained that each sphere has an intellect and a soul. And they also explained that each sphere is also divided according to the six directions into six divisions. So the sphere has right and left, front and back, above and below. So it is not far that it should have, according to each of these six divisions, a spirit that governs it. And the philosophers affirm the total: each sphere has an intellect and a soul, so the total becomes eight. And to this points His statement, Exalted: 'And your Lord's Throne will be borne above them on that Day by eight.' Then it is also not far that from each of those powerful, dominant spirits, branches and results should be generated. Allah knows best their number. And to this points His statement, Exalted: 'And you see the angels encircling the Throne, glorifying with praise of their Lord' — the verse.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is the first investigation in a set of metaphysical discussions. The author presents the philosophical view that each celestial sphere has an intellect and a soul. Additionally, each sphere has six directions (right, left, front, back, above, below), so it can have a governing spirit for each direction. The philosophers thus affirm that each sphere has one intellect, one soul, and six directional spirits, making a total of eight. The author connects this to the Quranic verse about eight bearers of the Throne on the Day of Judgment (69:17). He further suggests that from each of these powerful spirits, further branches and results are generated, quoting another verse about angels surrounding the Throne (39:75). The author presents this as a plausible interpretation supported by revelation.

Key Terms
عقل
نفس
الجهات الست
ثمانية
حملة العرش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is asserting that this philosophical model is the only correct interpretation of the Quranic verses.
Correction: The author presents it as a plausible explanation, using phrases like 'لا يبعد' and 'الله أعلم', indicating it is a possible interpretation, not a dogmatic assertion.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the total of 'eight' refer to in the context of each celestial sphere?
A) Eight angels
B) Eight spheres
C) The intellect, soul, and six directional spirits of a sphere
D) The eight planets in the solar system

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' view that each sphere has an intellect, soul, and six directional spirits totals eight, which the author connects to the eight bearers of the Throne in the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that each sphere has its own intellect and soul, do you feel a deeper sense of wonder at the intricate design of creation? How does this realization make you more mindful of the Creator's Majesty in your daily life? Commit to pausing today to reflect on one aspect of the universe that reminds you of His handiwork.
?
The author connects the eight components of the spheres to the Quranic verse about the Throne. Does this interpretation challenge or deepen your understanding of the Throne? Ask yourself: Are you open to exploring symbolic meanings in revelation, or do you prefer literal readings? How might this humility in interpretation affect your faith?
?
If every direction of a sphere is governed by a spirit, what does that imply about the pervasiveness of divine order in the cosmos? In your own life, where do you see evidence of such order—or where do you struggle to see it? Take a moment to thank Allah for the order you recognize and to seek His guidance where you find confusion.

The Hierarchy of Spirits and Souls
Classification of spirits from highest to lowest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchical classification of spirits and souls from the noblest celestial spirits to the lowest plant souls, and the analogy used to illustrate the gap between celestial and earthly spirits.

Core Idea
Spirits and souls are arranged in a descending hierarchy from celestial to earthly, with human spirits in between and great variation among them.

This passage presents a clear classification of spirits and souls, beginning with the highest celestial spirits and descending step by step to the lowest plant souls. The author uses a striking analogy comparing earthly spirits to celestial spirits as human bodies and hearts are to celestial bodies and planets. Understanding this hierarchy helps us grasp the immense gap between the spiritual and material realms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the order
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the starting point (the noblest) and the descent to the lowest. Notice the terminology used for each level.
Pay attention to the words 'الأشرف' and 'الأدون'.
2
Understand the analogy
Focus on the analogy comparing earthly spirits to celestial spirits. The author uses two comparisons: bodies and hearts. Consider what this tells us about the relationship.
The analogy highlights the extreme difference in rank.
3
Recognize the variation among human spirits
Notice the statement about human spirits having great disparity in nobility and baseness. This is a key point about the internal hierarchy within humans.
4
Learn the final categories
Identify the animal souls and plant souls as the last ranks. The passage concludes with 'here is the final rank.'
5
Reflect on the overall structure
Review the entire classification: celestial spirits, earthly spirits (by analogy), human spirits (with variation), animal souls, plant souls. Think about why this matters for theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأرواح، ابتدأ بالأشرف فالأشرف، منحطاً إلى الأدون فالأدون، حتى بلغت في آخر المراتب إلى الأرواح الأرضية. وهي بالنسبة إلى الأرواح الفلكية، كنسبة أجساد الناس إلى أجساد الأفلاك، وكنسبة القلوب المودعة في هذه الأجساد إلى الكواكب، التي هي جارية مجرى القلوب لتلك الأفلاك، ولما كانت نسبة الأجساد إلى الأجساد، والقلوب إلى القلوب، كالعدم بالنسبة إلى الوجود، فكذلك نسبة الأرواح السفلية إلى الأرواح العلوية. ثم إن الأرواح البشرية فيما بينها تفاوت شديد في الشرف والدناءة. وبعد الأرواح البشرية: النفوس الحيوانية، وبعدها: النفوس النباتية. وههنا آخر مراتب النفوس والأرواح.
The spirits: He began with the noblest and then the noblest, descending to the lower and lower until he reached in the last of the ranks to the earthly spirits. And they are, in relation to the celestial spirits, like the relation of the bodies of people to the bodies of the spheres, and like the relation of the hearts deposited in these bodies to the planets that run as hearts for those spheres. And since the relation of bodies to bodies and hearts to hearts is like non-existence relative to existence, so likewise is the relation of the lower spirits to the higher spirits. Then the human spirits among themselves have extreme disparity in nobility and baseness. And after the human spirits: the animal souls, and after them: the plant souls. And here is the final rank of souls and spirits.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents a hierarchical classification of spirits and souls. The author begins with the noblest spirits (celestial spirits) and descends step by step to the lowest, which are earthly spirits. He then draws an analogy: earthly spirits are to celestial spirits as human bodies are to celestial bodies, and as hearts in human bodies are to planets that serve as hearts for the celestial spheres. The relation of lower to higher is like non-existence to existence, emphasizing the immense gap. After this, he discusses human spirits, noting great variation among them in nobility and baseness. Then he lists the next lower categories: animal souls, then plant souls. The passage concludes by stating that this is the final rank of souls and spirits, indicating the end of this classification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which souls come after human spirits?
A) Celestial spirits
B) Earthly spirits
C) Animal souls
D) Plant souls

Key Takeaway
Spirits and souls are arranged in a descending hierarchy: celestial, earthly, human (with variation), animal, and plant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the extreme disparity among human spirits in nobility and baseness, where does your own soul stand today? Are you actively striving to elevate your spirit toward the celestial, or are you settling for a rank closer to the animal? What one change in your daily habits can you make starting today to lift your spirit?
?
If earthly spirits are higher than human spirits, what does that say about your attachment to this earthly world? Are you allowing material concerns to drag your spirit down from its potential human rank? How can you shift your focus from the earthly to the spiritual to ascend in this hierarchy?
?
The passage describes the relation of lower spirits to higher as like non-existence to existence. How does this perspective make you feel about your own shortcomings compared to the prophets and saints? Is your awareness of this gap motivating you to seek closeness to Allah, or is it causing despair? What is one concrete step you will take this week to bridge that gap, even slightly?

The Threefold Classification of the Self's Essence
Al-Razi classifies views on the self into three categories and elaborates the body view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's initial classification of views on the self into body, accident, or neither, and his preferred view that the self is this specific body.

Core Idea
The self is either a body, an accident, or neither — most theologians say it is this very body.

Al-Razi begins by grounding the discussion in the undeniable reality of the self—the 'I' that each of us knows directly. Yet when we ask about its essence, opinions diverge sharply. In this lesson, we explore his three-part classification and the first major view, which identifies the self with the physical body itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the three categories
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how al-Razi distinguishes body, accident, and neither.
The categories are exhaustive: every view falls into one.
2
Notice how al-Razi dismisses the 'outside body' view
He says no one has held that the self is a body outside the physical body. This eliminates one subcategory.
3
Understand why the 'this body' view is dominant
Al-Razi states that most people and most theologians adopt the view that the self is this specific body. Reflect on why this might be the default position.
This sets the stage for the detailed discussion of the interwoven body view in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التالي
في
تفصيل مذاهب الناس فيه
اعلم : أنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن هنا شيئاً يشير إليه كل أحد منا ، بقوله : ( أنا ) وبالحقيقة: أعرف المعارف، وأظهر الظاهرات : هو ما يشير إليه . كل أحد بقوله : ( أنا )
إلا أن العقلاء اختلفوا في [ أن ماهية (١) ] ذلك الشيء ، ما هو ؟
وضبط المذاهب فيه : أن يقال : إنه إما أن يكون جسماً ، أو عرضاً سارياً في الجسم ، أو لا جسماً ولا عرضاً(٢) سارياً في الجسم
·
أما القسم الأول : وهو أنه جسم . فذلك الجسم ، إما أن يكون هو هذا البدن ، وإما أن يكون جسماً مشابكاً لهذا البدن ، وإما أن يكون جسماً خارجاً عنه [ فأما القسم الثالث وهو أن تكون نفس الإنسان عبارة عن جسم خارج عن هذا البدن ، فهذا لم يقل به أحد (۳) ] وأما القسم الأول : وهو أن نفس الإنسان عبارة عن هذا البدن المعين ، والهيكل المخصوص . فهو قول جمهور الخلق ، وهو المختار عند أكثر المتكلمين . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن الإنسان (1) عبارة عن جسم مخصوص موجود في داخل هذا البدن . فالقائلون بهذا القول اختلفوا في تعيين نفس ذلك الجسم على وجوه : الأول : قال بعضهم : إنه عبارة عن الأخلاط الأربعة التي يتولد منها هذا البدن .
والثاني : قال بعضهم : إنه هو الدم. وهذا الإختلاف مبني على أصل آخر ، وهو أن الأطباء اختلفوا . فقال كثير منهم : إن هذا البدن متولد من مجموع الأخلاط الأربعة. وقال قوم من المحققين : إنه متولد من الدم فقط ، ثم تولد من هذا الأصل : الإختلاف في أن الإنسان . هل هو عبارة عن مجموع الأخلاط الأربعة ، أو عن الدم ؟
والقول الثالث : إنه عبارة عن الدم (۳) اللطيف، الذي يتولد من الجانب الأيسر من القلب ، وينفذ في الشريانات إلى سائر الأعضاء .
والقول الرابع : إنه هو الروح الذي يصعد من القلب إلى الدماغ ، ويتكيف بالكيفية الصالحة لقبول قوة الحفظ ، والفكر ، والذكر
القلب
والقول الخامس : وهو قول ( ابن الراوندي » - إنه جزء لا يتجزأ في
والقول السادس : وهو أنه جسم مخالف بالماهية للجسم ، الذي يتولد منه الأعضاء ، وذلك الجسم جسم نوراني علوي خفيف ، حي لذاته ، متحرك ، ينفذ في جواهر الأعضاء ، ويري فيها [ سريان النار في الفحم (٢) ] و سريان ماء الورد في الورد ، وسريان الدهن في السمسم . فما دامت هذه الأعضاء تبقى صالحة لقبول الآثار الفائضة عن هذا الجسم اللطيف الشريف ، وهو قوة الحس والحركة بالإرادة [ بقي (۳) ] مع ذلك الجسم ، مشابكاً لهذه الأعضاء ، وأفادها هذه الآثار الشريفة ، والقوى النورانية . وإذا فدت هذه
The following chapter on the detailed views of people regarding it. Know that we know by necessity that there is something here that each one of us points to by his saying 'I', and in reality the most known of known things and the most apparent of apparent things is what each one points to by his saying 'I'. However, the rational people differed about the essence (mahiyya) of that thing, what it is. The control of the views regarding it is that it be said: it is either a body (jism), or an accident (arad) permeating the body, or neither a body nor an accident permeating the body. As for the first division: that it is a body. That body is either this specific body, or a body interwoven with this body, or a body outside it. [As for the third division—that the human self is a body outside this body—no one has said this.] And as for the first division: that the human self is this specific body and particular structure, it is the saying of the majority of people, and it is the chosen view of most of the theologians (mutakallimun). And as for the second division: that the human is a specific body existing inside this body. Those who hold this view differed in specifying that body itself in several ways: The first: some said it is the four humors (akhlat) from which this body is generated. The second: some said it is the blood (dam). This difference is built upon another principle: the physicians differed; many of them said this body is generated from the totality of the four humors, while a group of investigators said it is generated from blood alone. Then from this principle arose the difference as to whether the human is the totality of the four humors or the blood. The third view: it is the subtle blood (dam latif) that is generated from the left side of the heart and passes through the arteries to the rest of the organs. The fourth view: it is the spirit (ruh) that ascends from the heart to the brain and takes on the quality suitable for receiving the power of memory, thought, and recollection. The fifth view: it is the saying of Ibn al-Rawandi—that it is an indivisible particle (juz' la yatajazza') in the heart. The sixth view: it is a body differing in essence from the body from which the organs are generated, and that body is a luminous, high, light, self-living, moving body, penetrating the substances of the organs and appearing in them like the penetration of fire in coal, the penetration of rose water in the rose, and the penetration of oil in sesame. As long as these organs remain fit to receive the overflowing effects from this subtle, noble body—which are the power of sensation and voluntary motion—[it remains] with that body, interwoven with these organs, and imparts to them these noble effects and luminous powers. And when these…
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a classification of views on the essence of the human self (nafs). Fakhr al-Din al-Razi begins by stating that each person knows by immediate necessity that there is a referent for the word 'I', which is the most evident of things. Yet philosophers and theologians have debated its true nature. He then presents a three-part classification: the self is either a body (jism), an accident (arad) permeating the body, or neither. He immediately focuses on the body view, subdividing it into three: the self is this specific body (the view of most people and theologians), a body interwoven with this body, or a body outside it. He dismisses the third as having no proponents. The second subcategory—that the self is a specific body inside the body—is further divided into six views. The first identifies it with the four humors. The second with blood, which depends on a prior medical dispute about the origin of the body. The third with subtle blood from the heart. The fourth with the spirit that ascends to the brain, preparing the faculties of memory, thought, and recall. The fifth from Ibn al-Rawandi is the indivisible atom in the heart. The sixth gives a long description: a luminous, noble body that pervades the organs like fire in coal, rose water in rose, or oil in sesame, imparting sensation and motion. Razi traces a web of interconnected medical and philosophical debates, showing the richness of classical discussions on the self.

Key Terms

بدن (badan)
جسم (jism)
عرض (arad)
ماهية (mahiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the self is purely spiritual, but al-Razi shows that many rational thinkers considered it a body.
Correction: Al-Razi's classification includes both material and immaterial options, but his initial focus is on the body view, which was widely held by theologians.

Checkpoint

A) The self is a body other than the physical body
B) The self is an accident permeating the body

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi classifies all views on the self into three broad categories and affirms that the majority of theologians identify the self with the physical body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Six Views on the Self as an Interwoven Body
Al-Razi enumerates six views on the self as an interwoven body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six detailed views of the self as a body interwoven with the physical body, including humoral, spiritual, and atomistic theories.

Core Idea
The self is a subtle body inside us — but what kind? Humors, blood, spirit, atom, or luminous substance?

After establishing that many thinkers consider the self a body interwoven with the physical body, al-Razi unfolds six specific theories. These range from the medical humors to a luminous, fire-like substance. This lesson explores each view and the medical debates underlying them.

Guided Path
1
Read the six views in order
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noting each view and its supporters. The passage enumerates them explicitly.
The first two views are tied to a medical dispute about the origin of the body.
2
Notice the medical disputes underlying the first two views
Al-Razi explains that the difference between the four humors and blood views stems from a deeper debate among physicians.
3
Reflect on the poetic analogy for the sixth view
The luminous body is compared to fire in coal, rose water in rose, and oil in sesame. Consider why such analogies are used to describe the self's relation to the body.
This view presents the self as a penetrating, life-giving substance.

والقول السادس : وهو أنه جسم مخالف بالماهية للجسم ، الذي يتولد منه الأعضاء ، وذلك الجسم جسم نوراني علوي خفيف ، حي لذاته ، متحرك ، ينفذ في جواهر الأعضاء ، ويري فيها [ سريان النار في الفحم (٢) ] و سريان ماء الورد في الورد ، وسريان الدهن في السمسم . فما دامت هذه الأعضاء تبقى صالحة لقبول الآثار الفائضة عن هذا الجسم اللطيف الشريف ، وهو قوة الحس والحركة بالإرادة [ بقي (۳) ] مع ذلك الجسم ، مشابكاً لهذه الأعضاء ، وأفادها هذه الآثار الشريفة ، والقوى النورانية . وإذا فدت هذه
The following chapter on the detailed views of people regarding it. Know that we know by necessity that there is something here that each one of us points to by his saying 'I', and in reality the most known of known things and the most apparent of apparent things is what each one points to by his saying 'I'. However, the rational people differed about the essence (mahiyya) of that thing, what it is. The control of the views regarding it is that it be said: it is either a body (jism), or an accident (arad) permeating the body, or neither a body nor an accident permeating the body. As for the first division: that it is a body. That body is either this specific body, or a body interwoven with this body, or a body outside it. [As for the third division—that the human self is a body outside this body—no one has said this.] And as for the first division: that the human self is this specific body and particular structure, it is the saying of the majority of people, and it is the chosen view of most of the theologians (mutakallimun). And as for the second division: that the human is a specific body existing inside this body. Those who hold this view differed in specifying that body itself in several ways: The first: some said it is the four humors (akhlat) from which this body is generated. The second: some said it is the blood (dam). This difference is built upon another principle: the physicians differed; many of them said this body is generated from the totality of the four humors, while a group of investigators said it is generated from blood alone. Then from this principle arose the difference as to whether the human is the totality of the four humors or the blood. The third view: it is the subtle blood (dam latif) that is generated from the left side of the heart and passes through the arteries to the rest of the organs. The fourth view: it is the spirit (ruh) that ascends from the heart to the brain and takes on the quality suitable for receiving the power of memory, thought, and recollection. The fifth view: it is the saying of Ibn al-Rawandi—that it is an indivisible particle (juz' la yatajazza') in the heart. The sixth view: it is a body differing in essence from the body from which the organs are generated, and that body is a luminous, high, light, self-living, moving body, penetrating the substances of the organs and appearing in them like the penetration of fire in coal, the penetration of rose water in the rose, and the penetration of oil in sesame. As long as these organs remain fit to receive the overflowing effects from this subtle, noble body—which are the power of sensation and voluntary motion—[it remains] with that body, interwoven with these organs, and imparts to them these noble effects and luminous powers. And when these…
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a classification of views on the essence of the human self (nafs). Fakhr al-Din al-Razi begins by stating that each person knows by immediate necessity that there is a referent for the word 'I', which is the most evident of things. Yet philosophers and theologians have debated its true nature. He then presents a three-part classification: the self is either a body (jism), an accident (arad) permeating the body, or neither. He immediately focuses on the body view, subdividing it into three: the self is this specific body (the view of most people and theologians), a body interwoven with this body, or a body outside it. He dismisses the third as having no proponents. The second subcategory—that the self is a specific body inside the body—is further divided into six views. The first identifies it with the four humors. The second with blood, which depends on a prior medical dispute about the origin of the body. The third with subtle blood from the heart. The fourth with the spirit that ascends to the brain, preparing the faculties of memory, thought, and recall. The fifth from Ibn al-Rawandi is the indivisible atom in the heart. The sixth gives a long description: a luminous, noble body that pervades the organs like fire in coal, rose water in rose, or oil in sesame, imparting sensation and motion. Razi traces a web of interconnected medical and philosophical debates, showing the richness of classical discussions on the self.

Key Terms
أخلاط (akhlat)
دم (dam)
روح (ruh)
جزء لا يتجزأ (juz' la yatajazza')
قلب (qalb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the self is a single, universally agreed-upon entity, but this passage shows multiple competing materialist theories.
Correction: Al-Razi records six distinct views among those who hold that the self is a body interwoven with the physical body, indicating significant disagreement within that camp.

Checkpoint

A) The self is the four humors
B) The self is blood
C) The self is an indivisible particle in the heart

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi records six distinct theories of the self as an interwoven body, ranging from humors to a luminous substance, showing the diversity of medieval philosophical and medical thought.

Sensory Localization and the Nature of the Soul
Argument from sensory localization to the soul's nature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how empirical observations of sensory localization lead to the conclusion that the soul is either the bodily structure or bodies interwoven with it.

Core Idea
Your senses prove your soul may be your body itself.

This passage presents a striking argument from everyday experience. The author invites us to notice that taste happens in the tongue, hearing in the ear, and thought in the brain. From these undeniable facts, he draws a conclusion about what the soul must be. Let's trace his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Notice the flow from observations to conclusion.
Pay attention to the phrase 'Know necessarily' – it signals certainty.
2
Identify the empirical observations
List the specific examples given: taste in tongue, hearing in ear, thought and imagination in brain, knowledge and perception in heart.
These are presented as self-evident facts.
3
Understand the logical leap
The author argues that since these perceptions are localized in organs, and the soul is described by these perceptions, the soul must also be in those organs.
This is the key step: the soul is the subject of these attributes.
4
Grasp the final disjunction
The conclusion is that the substance of the soul is either the bodily structure itself or bodies that permeate it.
This leaves open two materialist possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم بالضرورة : أن ذوق الطعام حاصل في اللسان وأن سماع الصوت حاصل بالأذن ، وكذا القول في سائر الإحساسات . وأيضاً : الإنسان يعلم بالبديهة أنه يجد الفكر والتخيل من جانب الدماغ ، ويجد العلم والإدراك من ناحية القلب . وجميع هذه الشواهد دالة على أن هذه الإدراكات مختصة بهذه الأعضاء فثبت : أن جوهر النفس [ جوهر مخصوص ] موصوف بهذه الصفات ، وثبت : أن الموصوف بهذه الصفات : هو هذه الأعضاء ، فوجب القطع بأن جوهر النفس إما هذه البنية ، وإما أجسام سارية في هذه البنية مشابكة لها .
Know necessarily that the taste of food occurs in the tongue, and that hearing of sound occurs by the ear, and likewise the statement concerning the rest of the sensations. Also, man knows intuitively that he finds thought and imagination from the side of the brain, and finds knowledge and perception from the side of the heart. And all these evidences indicate that these perceptions are specific to these organs. So it is established that the substance of the soul [is a specific substance] described by these attributes, and it is established that the described by these attributes is these organs, so it is necessary to decide that the substance of the soul is either this structure or bodies permeating this structure interwoven with it.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from sensory localization to prove that the soul is not separate from the body. It opens by stating a series of empirical facts: taste is located in the tongue, hearing in the ear, and similarly for other senses. Then it adds that mental activities like thought and imagination come from the brain, while knowledge and perception come from the heart. All these evidences show that perceptions are specific to particular bodily organs. From this, the author establishes two premises: (1) the essence of the soul (jawhar al-nafs) is described by these attributes (i.e., perception, thought, etc.), and (2) the entity described by these attributes is precisely these bodily organs. The conclusion is that the soul must be either the bodily structure itself or some bodies that permeate and interweave with that structure. This sets up a materialist view of the soul, which the author apparently defends against the idea of a separate, immaterial soul.

جوهر النفس
الإدراكات
الأعضاء
البنية
أجسام سارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage is a complete proof that the soul is material, ruling out any immaterial aspect.
Correction: The passage only concludes that the soul is either the bodily structure or interwoven bodies; it does not definitively exclude other possibilities at this stage.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion drawn from the localization of perceptions in specific organs?
A) The soul is a separate immaterial substance.
B) The soul is either the bodily structure or bodies interwoven with it.
C) The soul is identical to the brain.
D) The soul is a non-physical entity.

Key Takeaway
The soul, based on sensory localization, must be either the body's structure or bodies interwoven with it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If your soul is tied to that organ, what does that say about the intimacy between your body and your most personal sensations? Will you let this truth bring you closer to gratitude for your physical senses?
?
Your thoughts and ideas seem so abstract, yet the passage says they arise from your brain. Does it unsettle you that your deepest reflections might be nothing more than brain activity? If your soul is nothing without your body, how should you treat your body from now on?
?
The author leaves two possibilities: the soul is the body's structure or something interwoven within it. Neither lets the soul escape the body. If you truly believed this, how would it change your attitude toward physical acts of worship that involve your body?

The Fifth Argument: If the Soul Were Separate It Could Transfer Bodies
Fifth argument against separable soul with two refutations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument against a separate soul, how it refutes the claim of natural attachment, and why union is logically impossible.

Core Idea
If the soul were a separate manager, it could switch bodies—but it doesn't, so it must be the body itself.

This passage presents a decisive argument against the idea of the soul as a separate substance managing the body. Using the analogy of a sailor and ship, the author shows that a manager can always change its managed object. Since the soul cannot leave its body, it must be identical with it. The argument then refutes two objections: that the soul has a natural love for its body, and that the soul and body are somehow united.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of argument.
2
Understand the main analogy
Identify the analogy between soul-body and sailor-ship. Notice the implication: a manager can switch managed objects.
3
Examine the first objection and its refutation
The objector claims union. The author refutes by exploring three logical possibilities after union, showing each fails. Also note the material/immaterial gap.
4
Examine the second objection and its refutation
The objector claims natural love. The author argues that love of body is for pleasures, and all bodies equally provide pleasures, so no specific attachment is possible.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how both refutations support the conclusion that the soul is not separate but identical with the body.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن النفس لو كانت جوهراً مفارقاً للبدن ، ولا تعلق لها بالبدن ، إلا تعلق التدبير والتصرف، كتعلق الملاح بالسفينة ، والجمال بالمحمول، فكما أن الملاح يمكنه أن يترك تدبير هذه السفينة ، ويشتغل بتدبير سفينة أخرى ، وجب أن تتمكن النفس من أن تترك تدبير هذا البدن ، وتشتغل بتدبير بدن آخر . يعني أن مدير هذا البدن ، ينتقل إلى تدبير ذلك البدن ، ومدير ذلك البدن ينتقل إلى تدبير هذا البدن . ولما كان ذلك ممتنعاً ، علمنا : أن النفس ليست إلا هذا البدن . فإن قالوا : تم لا يجوز أن يقال : هذه النفس لها عشق طبيعي ، وشوق ذاتي ، إلى تدبير هذا البدن ، فلهذا السبب يمتنع إلى تدبير بدن آخر . والجواب عن الأول : إن القول بالإتحاد باطل ، مشهور البطلان . لأنهما بعد الإتحاد إن بقيا معاً . فهما اثنان لا واحد ، وإن عدما معاً ، وحدث ثالث فهذا ليس من الإتحاد في شيء وإن بقي أحدهما وعدم الآخر . فهذا أيضاً ليس بإتحاد ، لأن الموجود ليس نفس المعدوم . وأيضاً : البدن جسم مركب من الأخلاط المتضادة ، والأجزاء المتكاثرة ، والنفس جوهر مجرد عن الحجمية والعرضية ، فكيف يعقل أن يصير أحدهما عين الآخر ؟ والجواب عن الثاني : وهو قولكم : النفس المعينة لها عشق طبيعي ، وشوق ذاتي ، إلى تدبير هذا البدن. فنقول : هذا أيضاً ضعيف . لان عشق البدن لا يصح إلا [ ممن يقدر ] على الإلتذاذ بالمحسوسات ، وإلا على الإنتفاع بما في عالم الجسمانيات من المنافع والراحات . فأما أن تكون النفس عاشقة لهذه الآلهة بعينها ، مع أنه يمكنها تحصيل المقصود بسائر الآلات . فهذا محال . والدليل القاطع عليه : أن النفس متى جزمت وقطعت بأن تحصيل هذه اللذات والطيبات بهذا الطريق : ممكن . وبالطريق الثاني أيضاً : ممكن . وأنه لا رجحان لأحد الطريقين على الثاني في كثرة الفوائد والمنافع . فههنا يمتنع أن يكون للنفس ميل إلى أحد الطريقين بعينه دون الثاني . فكذا ههنا ، مطلوب النفس هو الإنتفاع باللذات الجسمانية والراحات الشهوانية. ولما كانت الأبدان بأسرها متساوية في الإفضاء إلى هذا المطلوب ، وجب أن تكون نسبة النفس إليها بأسرها على السوية. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، كان قولهم : إن النفس المعينة عاشقة لهذا البدن المعين عشقاً طبيعياً : قول باطل .
The fifth argument: If the soul were a substance separate from the body, having no attachment to the body except the attachment of management and disposal, like the attachment of the sailor to the ship and the camel-driver to the load, then just as the sailor can leave the management of this ship and busy himself with the management of another ship, it would be necessary for the soul to be able to leave the management of this body and busy itself with the management of another body. That is, the manager of this body transfers to the management of that body, and the manager of that body transfers to the management of this body. Since that is impossible, we know that the soul is nothing but this body. If they say: 'But it is not permissible to say that this soul has a natural love and essential longing for the management of this body, and for this reason it is prevented from (managing) another body.' The answer to the first (part of the objection): Indeed, the claim of union is false, well-known in its falsehood. Because after union, if both remain together, then they are two, not one; if both cease and a third arises, then this is not union at all; if one remains and the other ceases, then this is also not union, because the existent is not the same as the non-existent. Also, the body is a compound substance composed of opposing humors and numerous parts, while the soul is an immaterial substance free from spatiality and accidentalness, so how can it be intelligible for one to become the same as the other? And the answer to the second (part): which is your saying: 'This particular soul has a natural love and essential longing for the management of this body.' We say: This is also weak. Because love of the body is not valid except from one who is capable of taking pleasure in sensibles, and of benefiting from the benefits and comforts in the world of corporeal things. But for the soul to be in love with these very instruments, while it can attain the desired end through other instruments, this is impossible. The decisive proof of this is: When the soul is certain and decides that obtaining these pleasures and delights by this path is possible, and by the second path also possible, and there is no preference of one path over the other in abundance of benefits and advantages, then it is impossible for the soul to have an inclination towards one specific path to the exclusion of the other. So here too, the desired object of the soul is to benefit from bodily pleasures and sensual comforts. Since all bodies are equal in leading to this desired object, the soul's relation to all of them must be equal. When that is the case, their saying 'This particular soul is naturally in love with this particular body' is a false statement.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument against the idea that the soul is a separate substance managing the body. The argument is an analogy: if the soul were like a sailor managing a ship, then it should be able to leave one body and manage another, just as a sailor can switch ships. Since we observe that this does not happen, the soul must be the body itself. The author then considers an objection: one might say that the soul has a natural love for its particular body, preventing it from leaving. The answer is twofold. First, the objection implies a union between soul and body, but the concept of union is shown to be logically incoherent. The author breaks it down: if two things unite, either both remain (so they are two, not one), both vanish and a third arises (not union), or one remains and the other vanishes (the remaining is not the other). Therefore, union is impossible. Additionally, the body is composite and material, while the soul is immaterial, so they cannot become identical. Second, the claim of natural love is refuted by arguing that love of a body implies desire for the pleasures it provides. But if the soul could attain those pleasures through any body, then it would have no special attachment to one particular body. Since all bodies are equal in providing sensual pleasures, the soul should have no preference. Therefore, the claim of natural love for a specific body is false.

Key Terms
جوهر مفارق
تعلق التدبير
عشق طبيعي
الاتحاد
جوهر مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies any distinction between soul and body.
Correction: The argument refutes the claim that the soul is a separate substance that can manage any body; it does not deny that the soul is a distinct entity but argues it is not separable in that way.

Checkpoint
What is the main analogy used in the fifth argument against a separate soul?
A) A carpenter and his tool.
B) A sailor and his ship.
C) A driver and his car.
D) A king and his kingdom.

Key Takeaway
The soul cannot be a separate manager because it cannot change bodies; the idea of natural love for a specific body is refuted by the equal availability of pleasures in all bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim your soul is 'yours' and uniquely attached to this body. But if all bodies provide equal pleasures, why do you feel this attachment? Is it merely habit or something deeper? What would it mean for your identity if the soul could just as well manage another body?
?
The author says love of the body is only for its pleasures. Think of one physical pleasure you enjoy. Could you just as easily enjoy it through a different body? If not, what does that say about your belief in the soul's separability?
?
You probably act as if your soul is distinct from your body, directing it like a manager. But if this argument is true, you are nothing but this very body. How would your daily choices change if you truly believed that there is no separate soul managing your body?

Ibn Sina's Poem on the Soul: Analysis of Its Three Sections
Ibn Sina's poem on the soul and its threefold analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three thematic sections of Ibn Sina's poem on the soul and the philosophical issues raised by the commentator.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's poem is divided into three parts: imprisonment, purpose, and denial of reincarnation.

Ibn Sina's famous 'Ayniyya' poem on the soul is presented here along with a modern scholarly analysis that divides it into three thematic sections. You will read the Arabic poem, examine the commentary, and consider the philosophical tensions between the poem and Ibn Sina's other works.

Guided Path
1
Read the full poem
Begin by reading the Arabic poem from start to finish. Notice the imagery of descent, weeping, and yearning for a lost home. The poem uses classical Arabic poetic conventions to express the soul's condition.
The poem may contain OCR errors; focus on the overall themes.
2
Identify the three sections
The analysis divides the poem into three sections: verses 1-14 (imprisonment), 15-18 (purpose of union), and 19-20 (denial of reincarnation). Locate these divisions in the poem and see how the commentator characterizes each.
The analysis is given in the paragraph after the poem.
3
Examine the Platonic echo
The commentator notes that the first section echoes Plato's theory of the soul's pre-existence and descent. But Ibn Sina differs by not including sin or reincarnation. Consider how the poem might be adapting Platonic ideas.
4
Resolve the tension with Ibn Sina's other works
Ibn Sina does not affirm the soul's pre-existence in his philosophical works. The commentator suggests interpreting 'the highest place' as the Active Intellect. Reflect on how this resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هبطت اليك من المحل الأرفع
ورفاه محجوبة عن كل مقلة عارف
وصلت على كره إليك، وربما
ذات تعزز وتمنع
التي سفرت، ولم تتبرقع
فراقك، وهي ذات تفجع
أنفت وما ألفت، قبلها واصلت
ست مجاورة الخراب وأظنها
نسيت عهوداً بالحمى ومنازلاً
بفراقها لم تقنع
حتى إذا اتصلت بهاء هبوطها
عن ميم مركزها، بذات الأجـرع
علقت بها ثاء الثقيل فاصبحت
بين المعالم والطول الخضع؟
تبكي إذا ذكرت عهوداً بالحمى
مع تهمي، ولما ولما تقلع
وتظل ساجعة على الدمن التي
درست بتكرار الرياح الأربع
إذا عاقها الشرك الكثيف وصدها
قفص عن الأوج الفيح المربع
حق إذا قرب المير إلى الحمى
ودنا الرحيل إلى الفضاء وغدت
مفارقة لكل مخلف عنها، حليف الترب، غير مش صجعت
وقد كشف الغطاء فابصرت
ليس يدرك بالعيون المجع
وغدت تفرد فوق ذروة شاهق
علم يرفع كل من رفع
فلأي شيء أهبطت مع شامخ عال
إلى قمر الحضيض الأوضع ؟
إن كان أمبطها الإله لحكمة
طوبت عن الفطن اللبيب الأروع
فهبوطها - إن كان ضربة لازب
لتكون مسامعة بما لم وتعود
عالمة بكل حقيقة في العالمين - فخرقها لم يرقع
وهي التي قطع الزمان طريقها
فكانها برق تألق بالحمى
لقد غربت بغیر انطوى
فكأنه لم يلم

وتنقسم هذه القصيدة ثلاثة أقسام:
1 - سجن النفس: هبطت النفس من عل، وحلت في الجسد شقية سجينة. وهي إن ارتاحت إلى سكناه بعض الراحة، فحنينها أبداً إلى عالمها، وهناؤ هنا إذ تعود إليه مغردة هانئة (بيت 1 - 14).
2 - غاية التحاد النفس بالجسد: لم حلت النفس في الجد؟ التعرف كل شيء؟ إنها لم تعرف. (بيت 15 - 18).
3 - إنكار تناسخ النفس: ولن تعود النفس بالتناسخ، لكي تكمل معرفتها (بيت 19 - 20).
إننا نرى، في القسم الأول، صدى نظرية أفلاطون القائلة بوجود النفس قبل البدن، وهبوطها من عالم المثل إلى عالم الحسن. ولكن أفلاطون يهبط النفس إلى الجسد الجناية صدرت عنها، ويقضي عليها بالتناسخ تكفيراً عن إنمها، وهذا ما لا يتفق وباقي القصيدة. فهل يكون ابن سينا بتر نظرية افلاطون؟ ثم إن ابن سينا لا يسلم، في باقي كتبه بوجود النفس قبل البدن. فهل يمكن أن يسلم به هنا؟ وعليه نرى أن تجعل من المحل الأرفع، العقل الفعال، فيتلاءم ما في القصيدة مع باقي كتب ابن.
(من كتاب النفس البشرية عند ابن سينا، للدكتور ألبير نصر نادر)
You descended to me from the highest place, veiled from every knowing eye. You arrived unwillingly, and perhaps... [poem continues with themes of separation, weeping, and yearning]. And this poem is divided into three sections: 1 - Imprisonment of the soul: The soul descended from on high and settled in the body, wretched and imprisoned. Though it finds some rest in its dwelling, its longing is always for its world, and its bliss is when it returns to it singing joyfully (verses 1-14). 2 - The purpose of the soul's union with the body: Why did the soul settle in the body? To know everything? It did not know (verses 15-18). 3 - Denial of reincarnation: The soul will not return through reincarnation to complete its knowledge (verses 19-20). We see, in the first section, an echo of Plato's theory of the soul's pre-existence and its descent from the world of ideas to the world of sense. But Plato makes the soul descend due to a sin, and condemns it to reincarnation as expiation for its sin, which does not agree with the rest of the poem. So did Ibn Sina truncate Plato's theory? Moreover, Ibn Sina does not affirm the pre-existence of the soul in his other books. Can he affirm it here? Therefore, we see that we should make 'the highest place' refer to the Active Intellect, so that what is in the poem aligns with the rest of Ibn Sina's books. (From the book 'The Human Soul according to Ibn Sina' by Dr. Albert Nasri Nader.)
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents an analysis of Ibn Sina's famous poem on the soul (the 'Ayniyya'), dividing it into three thematic sections and discussing its philosophical implications. The poem itself describes the soul's descent from a high place into the body, its suffering, longing, and eventual realization. The analysis classifies the verses: first, the soul's imprisonment in the body (verses 1-14); second, the question of why the soul united with the body and whether it gained knowledge (verses 15-18); third, the rejection of reincarnation (verses 19-20). The commentator then notes the Platonic echo in the first section but points out that Plato's doctrine involves punishment and reincarnation, which is absent here. The commentator raises a problem: Ibn Sina elsewhere does not believe in the soul's pre-existence. To resolve it, the commentator suggests interpreting 'the highest place' as the Active Intellect, aligning the poem with Ibn Sina's broader philosophy. This passage is a modern scholarly analysis, not a traditional kalam proof, but it illustrates how classical poems were used in philosophical discussions.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
التجرد
القصيدة العينية
سجن النفس
التناسخ
العقل الفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina believed in the pre-existence of the soul as a separate substance before the body.
Correction: The poem might be metaphorical; the 'highest place' is likely the Active Intellect, not a pre-existent soul, aligning with Ibn Sina's philosophical works where he denies the soul's pre-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the analysis, how many sections is Ibn Sina's poem divided into?
A) Two sections
B) Three sections
C) Four sections
D) Five sections

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's poem on the soul is analyzed into three sections, and the commentator resolves the apparent belief in pre-existence by interpreting the 'highest place' as the Active Intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina's poem portrays the soul as a prisoner yearning for its true home. If you truly believed that your soul is a guest in this world, how would your daily priorities change? What attachment would you need to break today?
?
The commentator argues that the soul's descent is not due to sin but for a hidden wisdom. Are you living as if your existence here has a specific purpose from God, or are you drifting aimlessly? What one thing will you do this week to align your life with that purpose?
?
Ibn Sina denies reincarnation; this life is your only chance to purify your soul. If you knew there was no second chance, what would you do differently starting tomorrow?

The Universal is Humanity, Not Knowledge of Humanity
Response to the second point, clarifying the universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second point is conceded but does not undermine the claim that humanity itself is a universal.

Core Idea
The universal is humanity itself, not the knowledge of humanity.

The author responds to a concession regarding the second point. He clarifies that the universal shared among humans is the essence of humanity, not the mental concept of it. Therefore, external accidents affecting knowledge of humanity do not affect the universality of humanity itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the author's key assertion.
Notice the concession and the clarification.
2
Identify the universal
Identify what the author says is the universal common among human individuals.
It is humanity, not knowledge of humanity.
3
Understand the implication
Understand why the concession does not harm the author's position: the universal is not the mental form but the essence.
External accidents to knowledge do not affect the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني : فهو مسلّم إلا أنه لا يفيد مقصودكم ، لأن الأمر الكلي المشترك فيه بين الأشخاص الإنسانية ، هو الإنسانية ، لا العلم بالإنسانية . وإذا كان كذلك ، لم يلزم من كون العلم بالإنسانية مقرونا بالعوارض الخارجية : حصول القدح في قولنا : الإنسانية من حيث هي هي : أمر كلي مشترك فيها . وتمام الكلام في هذا البحث : مذكور في باب والجزئي من المنطق ، والإلهيات . السؤال الثاني : إن هذا الإشكال وارد عليكم أيضاً وذلك لأن الصورة الكلية التي تثبتونها وتزعمون أنها حالة في النفس ، فهي صورة شخصية موصوفة بعوارض شخصية ، أعني حلولها في تلك النفس وحدوثها في وقت معين ، ومقارنتها لسائر الصور العقلية ، ولسائر الأعراض الحاصلة في جوهر النفس . فثبت أن هذه الصورة العقلية وإن كانت حالة في جوهر ، ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني ، فهي غير مجردة عن العوارض الخارجية . فإن قالوا : المراد بكونها مجردة : أنها نظراً إلى ماهيتها ، مع قطع النظر عن العوارض المذكورة مجردة فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن تكون الصورة الحالة في المحل الجسماني تكون مجردة ، بمعنى أنها بالنظر إلى حقيقتها وماهيتها مع قطع النظر عن العوارض تكون مجردة وكلية ؟
As for the second: it is conceded but it does not serve your purpose, because the universal common among human individuals is humanity, not knowledge of humanity. If that is so, then the fact that knowledge of humanity is accompanied by external accidents does not entail any defect in our statement: 'Humanity as such is a universal common among them.' The complete discussion of this topic is mentioned in the chapter on the universal and the particular from logic and theology. The second question: This objection applies to you as well, because the universal form that you affirm and claim that it is a state in the soul is a particular form described by particular accidents, namely its inherence in that soul, its occurrence at a specific time, its association with other mental forms and with other accidents occurring in the substance of the soul. So it is established that this mental form, even if it inheres in a substance that is neither a body nor bodily, is not free from external accidents. If they say: What is meant by its being free is that it is free with respect to its essence, disregarding the mentioned accidents, we say: Why may not the form in a bodily subject be free and universal in the sense of being considered in terms of its reality and essence, disregarding accidents?
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage begins by responding to a second point conceded by the opponent but deemed irrelevant. The author clarifies that the universal common among human individuals is humanity itself, not the knowledge of humanity. Therefore, the fact that knowledge of humanity is accompanied by external accidents does not invalidate the statement that humanity as such is a universal. The discussion is then referred to logic and theology. Following this, a new objection is presented under 'the second question'. The objector argues that the universal form posited as existing in the soul is itself a particular form with particular accidents like inherence, time, and association. Thus, it is not free from external circumstances. If the opponent claims that 'freedom' refers to the essence disregarding accidents, the author retorts: if that is allowed, then why cannot a form in a bodily subject also be considered free and universal in the same sense? This challenges the immateriality argument.

Key Terms
كلي
جزئي
عارض
إنسانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The universal common among humans is the mental concept or knowledge of humanity.
Correction: According to the author, the universal is humanity itself, not knowledge of humanity. Accidents affecting knowledge do not affect the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the universal common among human individuals?
A) The knowledge of humanity
B) Humanity itself
C) The mental form
D) The bodily form

Key Takeaway
Humanity as such is a universal shared among individuals, independent of the mental concept of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat people based on their external attributes or their essential humanity? Reflect on a recent interaction where you may have focused on accidents rather than the universal. What will you do tomorrow to see the essence in someone you usually overlook?
?
If humanity as such is universal and independent of accidents, how does that challenge your tendency to divide people by race, status, or appearance? What specific step will you take this week to honor the common humanity in someone different from you?
?
When you catch yourself judging others by their circumstances, remember that their humanity remains untouched. Choose one person you have misjudged and make a concrete effort to connect with them purely as a human being. What will that look like?

The Objection: Universal Form in the Soul is Particular
Objection and reply regarding the particularity of the universal mental form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the universal form in the soul is particular and the author's reply that freedom from accidents can be considered by essence.

Core Idea
If a form can be considered universal by essence, then even bodily forms could be considered universal.

The objector argues that the universal form affirmed in the soul is itself a particular form with particular accidents. The author responds by considering whether universality can be defined by essence disregarding accidents, and then asks why the same cannot apply to bodily forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection that the universal form in the soul is particular due to its accidents.
The objector lists accidents like inherence, time, and association.
2
Read the reply
Read the author's reply that if freedom from accidents is defined by essence, then why not allow bodily forms to be considered universal similarly?
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Analyze the logic
Understand how the author turns the objection back on the opponent by showing their own definition leads to an unwanted consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the second: it is conceded but it does not serve your purpose, because the universal common among human individuals is humanity, not knowledge of humanity. So it is established that this mental form, even if it inheres in a substance that is neither a body nor bodily, is not free from external accidents. If they say: What is meant by its being free is that it is free with respect to its essence, disregarding the mentioned accidents, we say: Why may not the form in a bodily subject be free and universal in the sense of being considered in terms of its reality and essence, disregarding accidents?

?

?

Proof that the Rational Faculty is Not Seated in a Body
Conditional argument against the rational faculty being in a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational faculty cannot be seated in a body, according to the author's conditional argument.

Core Idea
The rational faculty cannot reside in a body because that would make its perception of it either perpetual or impossible.

This passage presents a rigorous conditional argument. The author first rules out direct imprinting of a form on the rational faculty, then shows that the only remaining possibilities for perception lead to contradiction with our actual awareness of our own heart and brain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the logical structure: a conditional followed by two possibilities.
The argument is tightly structured; pay attention to the 'if...then' logic.
2
Identify the two perception scenarios
The author argues that if the rational faculty were in a body, its perception of that body would be either always (due to mere presence) or never (if presence is insufficient). Identify these two branches in the text.
Both branches are shown to be impossible, leading to the conclusion.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Follow how the author falsifies the consequent (always or never) by appeal to our common experience of sometimes being aware of our heart/brain and sometimes not. Then note that the false consequent forces the antecedent to be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن تلك الصورة إذا حلت في القوة العاقلة الحالة في تلك الآلة ، فحينئذ يلزم كون تلك الصورة حالة في تلك الآلة ، فيلزم : اجتماع صورتين متماثلتين في تلك المادة ، والجمع بين المثلين: محال ، ولما بطل هذا القسم الأول ، وهو أن يقال : إن القوة العاقلة ، لو أدركت آلتها لكان إدراكها لتلك الآلة عبارة عن نفس حضور تلك الآلة عند القوة العاقلة . وهذا القدر من الحضور ، إن كان كافياً في حصول الإدراك وجب حصول الإدراك دائماً . وإن لم يكن كافياً ، امتنع حصول الإدراك في شيء من الأوقات . لأنه لو حصل في بعض الأوقات، لكان ذلك بسبب أمر زائد على مجرد حضور صورة الألة ، وذلك الزائد يجب أن يكون عبارة عن حصول صورة مساوية لها في القوة الناطقة ، بناء على القاعدة المشهورة من أن إدراك الشيء عبارة عن حضور صورة مساوية للمعقول في العاقل : وقد بينا أن ذلك محال . فثبت بهذا البيان : أن القوة العاقلة لو كانت حالة في جسم ، كقلب أو دماغ ، لوجب أن تكون مدركة لذلك الجسم دائماً ، أو تكون ممتنعة الإدراك دائماً . أما بيان أن هذا التالي محال : فلانا نعلم بالضرورة : أنا في بعض الأوقات نستحضر صورة القلب والدماغ في عقولنا ، وفي بعض الأوقات تكون غافلين عن هذا الإستحضار . فثبت بما ذكرنا : صحة الشرطية ، وثبت : فساد التالي ، فيلزم فساد المقدم
Because if that form (ṣūra) is imprinted on the rational faculty (al-quwwa al-ʿāqila) which is seated in that instrument (āla), then it necessarily follows that that form is seated in that instrument, so there would be two similar forms in that substrate, and combining two similars is impossible. And since this first scenario is invalid, it could be said: The rational faculty, if it were to perceive its instrument, its perception of that instrument would consist in the very presence (ḥuḍūr) of that instrument to the rational faculty. This amount of presence, if it were sufficient for perception to occur, then perception would necessarily be perpetual. And if it were not sufficient, then perception would be impossible at any time. For if it occurred at some times, that would be due to an additional factor beyond mere presence of the form of the instrument, and that additional factor would have to consist in the occurrence of a form equal to it in the rational faculty, based on the well-known principle that the perception of a thing consists in the presence of a form equal to the intelligible (maʿqūl) to the intellect (ʿāqil): but we have shown that this is impossible. So it is established by this exposition that if the rational faculty were seated in a body, such as a heart or brain, it would have to be perceiving that body perpetually, or be permanently incapable of perception. As for the proof that this consequent is false: we know by necessity that at some times we summon the form of the heart and brain in our minds, and at other times we are heedless of this summoning. So by what we have mentioned, the conditional is valid and the consequent is false, so the antecedent must be false.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by conditional argument. The author begins by recalling a previous point: if a form (of the instrument) were imprinted on the rational faculty, then the form would also be in the instrument, causing two similar forms in one substrate—impossible. So the first scenario (direct imprinting) is invalid. Then he sets up a conditional: if the rational faculty were in a body (instrument), then perceiving that body would have to be either through mere presence (always perceiving) or through something additional (requiring a form, already proven impossible), so either always or never perceiving. But we know by necessity that we are sometimes aware of our heart/brain and sometimes not, so the consequent (always or never) is false; therefore the antecedent (that the rational faculty is in a body) must be false. The argument is tight: the two possibilities are exhaustive, both lead to absurdities or contradiction with experience, so the original premise is negated.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the rational faculty can be seated in the brain while the perception of the brain occurs through some other mechanism.
Correction: The author argues that the very idea of the faculty being in a substrate leads to impossibility regardless of mechanism, because perception would require either perpetual awareness (contradicted by experience) or an additional form (already proven impossible).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the 'always perceive' option impossible?
A) Because two similar forms cannot coexist in the same substrate.
B) Because we know by necessity that we sometimes perceive the heart and brain and sometimes do not.
C) Because the rational faculty is immaterial and cannot perceive matter.
D) Because the presence of a form is insufficient for perception without additional conditions.

Key Takeaway
The rational faculty is not seated in a body because that would lead to an absurdity: either perpetual awareness of our own organs or total ignorance, neither of which matches our experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
You have experienced moments of deep concentration and moments of distraction. Does your awareness of your own existence feel dependent on your body? If the rational soul is not seated in the heart or brain, then where is your true 'self'? How does this truth affect your responsibility for your thoughts and actions?

What does this tell you about the independence of your intellect from matter? Will you live today more mindful of the immaterial reality of your soul, or will you continue to act as if you are only a body?

Four Arguments that Perception is Distinct from a Form
Four arguments that perception is not the presence of a form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four independent proofs that perception is not the same as the mere presence of a form, but rather an active relational state.

Core Idea
Perception is an additional relational state, not the presence of a form.

The author has already argued that a form stripped of quiddity cannot occur in the intellect. Now, even if we concede that such a form could be present, he insists that perception cannot be identical to that form. He presents four clear arguments from physical and logical grounds to establish that perception is a distinct, relational state. Let us examine each argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the overall flow. Notice the objection and the four enumerated arguments.
The passage begins with a concession before launching into the proofs.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument uses the example of a black body: blackness is present but perception of blackness is not. This shows perception is not mere presence. The response to a potential objection is also included.
3
Analyze the second and third arguments
The second argument uses imagination (khayāl) as a storehouse of forms without awareness. The third argument uses the optics of the eye: images impress on both lenses but sight is single. Both prove perception is not the same as physical impression.
The third argument relies on Avicenna's anatomy.
4
Reflect on the fourth argument and conclusion
The fourth argument states that perception is known by necessity to be a relational state, so it cannot be identical to a form. The passage ends with a suggestion about the rational faculty being in a body yet having an added state.
This final point opens the door for the author's own positive view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإعتراض : إنا قد ذكرنا الدلائل الكثيرة على أن حصول صورة مسلوبة في تمام الماهية للمعقول في جوهر العاقل محال ، فلا نعيد تلك الدلائل . ثم لئن سلمنا ذلك ، لكن حصول تلك الصورة ، يكون شرطاً لحصول الإدراك والشعور ، فإما أن يقال : إن الإدراك عبارة عن عين تلك الصورة ؛ وهذا لا يقول عاقل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن الجسم الموصوف بالسواد يكون السواد حاصلاً فيه ، وإدراك السواد غير حاصل فيه ، فإدراك السواد مغاير لحصول ماهية السواد . فإن قالوا : حصول الماهية إنما يكون إدراكاً لها، إذا كان ذلك الشيء الذي حضرت الماهية عنده : قوة مدركة ، فإذا كان الجسم جماداً ، فقد فات هذا الشرط ، فيفوت المشروط فنقول في الجواب : إن كان الإدراك عبارة عن مجرد الماهية عنده . فالمدرك يجب أن يكون عبارة عن الشيء الذي حضرت الماهية عنده . فوجب أن يكون الجماد مدركاً . وإن قلنا : إنه ليس كلما حضرت الماهية عنده ، بل قد يكون مدركاً لها ، وقد لا يكون . فهذا يقتضي أن يكون الإدراك حالة مغايرة لمجرد الحضور . وهو المطلوب . الثاني : وهو إن الخيال الذي هو خزانة الحس المشترك ، قد حصلت الصور الجسمانية عنده ، مع أن قوة الإدراك غير حاصلة ، وذلك يدل على قولنا . الثالث : إن أشباح المبصرات تنطبع في كل واحد من الجليدتين ، والإدراك غير حاصل هناك ، وإلا لزم أن يدرك الإنسان : المرئي الواحد شيئين . لأنه انطبع في كل واحد من الجليدتين صورة على حدة . بل الحق أن الإنطباع يحصل في الجليدتين . إلا أن قوة الإبصار إنما تحصل عند ملتقى العصبتين المجوفتين . هكذا قاله الشيخ في كتاب : الشفاء ، وهذا يدل على أن الإدراك غير الانطباع . الرابع : وهو أنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن الإدراك والشعور والإبصار : أحوال نسبية إضافية ، فيمتنع أن يكون عبارة عن مجرد حضور تلك الماهية . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : القوة العقلية حالة في جسم مخصوص ، ثم إن القوة الناطقة قد تحصل لها الحالة الإضافية المسماة بالشعور
And the objection: We have already presented many proofs that the occurrence of a form stripped completely of the quiddity (māhiyya) of the intelligible in the substance of the intellect is impossible, so we will not repeat those proofs. Then even if we concede that, still the occurrence of that form is a condition for the occurrence of perception (idrāk) and awareness (shuʿūr). So it must be said: Is perception the very same as that form? No rational person would say that. Evidence for this includes several points: First: A body described as black has blackness (sawād) present in it, but perception of blackness is not present in it. So perception of blackness is different from the occurrence of the essence of blackness. If they say: The occurrence of the essence is perception of it only when the thing at which the essence is present is a perceiving power; if the body is inanimate, this condition is absent, so the conditioned is absent. Then we reply: If perception is merely the presence of the essence, then the perceiver (mudrik) must be the thing at which the essence is present. So an inanimate thing would have to be a perceiver. And if we say that not whenever an essence is present at something does it perceive it—sometimes it perceives it, sometimes not—then this implies that perception is a state (ḥāla) distinct from mere presence. That is what we wanted to establish. Second: The imagination (khayāl), which is the treasury of the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak), has bodily forms present in it, yet the power of perception is not present therein; this supports our claim. Third: The images (ashbāḥ) of visible objects are impressed (tanṭabiʿ) on each of the two lenses (al-jalidatayn), yet perception is not present there; otherwise, a human being would perceive a single visible object as two things, because an image is impressed on each lens separately. Rather, the truth is that the impression occurs on the two lenses, but the power of sight (quwwat al-ibṣār) occurs only at the meeting of the two hollow nerves (al-ʿaṣabatayn al-mujawwafatayn). This is what the Shaykh said in the Book of Healing (Kitāb al-Shifāʾ), and this shows that perception is not the same as impression. Fourth: We know by necessity that perception, awareness, and sight are relative, relational states (aḥwāl nisbiyya iḍāfiyya), so they cannot be the mere presence of that essence. And once this is established, we say: Why is it not permissible to say that the rational faculty is seated in a specific body, and then the rational power (al-quwwa al-nāṭiqa) may acquire the additional state called awareness?
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by four arguments. The objector says: We already proved that a form stripped of quiddity cannot occur in the intellect; but even if we allow it, that form is only a condition for perception, not perception itself. The author then refutes the idea that perception is identical to the form, providing four arguments. (1) A black body has blackness but not perception of blackness; if someone says the body lacks the active power, the reply is that if perception were mere presence, the inanimate would be a perceiver; if not, perception is an added state. (2) Imagination contains bodily forms without awareness, showing perception is separate. (3) Images impress on each lens, but sight is single and occurs at the meeting of nerves, proving perception is not the impression. (4) Perception is known by necessity to be a relative relation, not a mere presence. The passage concludes that the rational faculty could be in a body and still have the additional state of awareness.
