Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perception is the same as having a form present in the mind or sense organ.
Correction: Perception is an active relational state that may require a form as a condition, but it is not identical to the form's presence. The author shows this through physical examples and logical necessity.

Checkpoint

B) Because if perception were the impression, a single object would be perceived as two.
C) Because the lenses are hollow and do not contain the power of sight.
D) Because the impression occurs only at the meeting of the nerves.

Key Takeaway
Perception is a relational state distinct from the mere presence of a form, as demonstrated by four independent arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If perception is not a passive reception of forms but an active relational state, then how are you responsible for what you perceive? Is there a truth you have known (form present) but never truly perceived (active awareness) – and what would it take to change that today?
?
The fourth argument says we know by necessity that perception is relative. When have you treated your own perception as absolute and judged others unjustly? How can you cultivate humility about your perceptions from now on?
?
Consider the example of the black body: blackness present but no perception. Are there areas of your life where you have the 'form' of knowledge (e.g., from reading this lesson) but lack the active awareness that transforms? What one step will you take to move from form to perception?

Four Proofs that Perception is Not the Mere Presence of the Form
Author gives four proofs that perception is not merely the presence of the form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four rational proofs demonstrating that perception is not merely the presence of the form, but an additional relational state.

Core Idea
Perception is a relational state, not the form's mere presence.

The author begins by dismissing the view that perception is identical to the form as irrational. He then presents four distinct arguments that collectively refute this identity theory. These proofs draw on examples from bodies, imagination, vision, and the nature of relational states. The passage ends with a surprising remark about Ibn Sina's endorsement of a weak argument.

Guided Path
1
Follow the first proof with objection and response
Read the first proof which uses the example of a black body. Notice the objection and the author's reply that perception must be something extra beyond presence.
See how the argument shifts the burden.
2
Examine the second and third proofs
The second proof uses imagination; the third uses optics. Both show perception absent where forms are present.
3
Understand the fourth proof
The fourth proof argues that perception is a relative state, not a mere presence.
4
Reflect on the concluding argument and critique
The author provides a final positive argument and criticizes Ibn Sina for calling such a weak argument a great demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشعور ، فإما أن يقال : إن الإدراك عبارة عن عين تلك الصورة ؛ وهذا لا يقول عاقل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن الجسم الموصوف بالسواد يكون السواد حاصلاً فيه ، وإدراك السواد غير حاصل فيه ، فإدراك السواد مغاير لحصول ماهية السواد . فإن قالوا : حصول الماهية إنما يكون إدراكاً لها، إذا كان ذلك الشيء الذي حضرت الماهية عنده : قوة مدركة ، فإذا كان الجسم جماداً ، فقد فات هذا الشرط ، فيفوت المشروط فنقول في الجواب : إن كان الإدراك عبارة عن مجرد الماهية عنده . فالمدرك يجب أن يكون عبارة عن الشيء الذي حضرت الماهية عنده . فوجب أن يكون الجماد مدركاً . وإن قلنا : إنه ليس كلما حضرت الماهية عنده ، بل قد يكون مدركاً لها ، وقد لا يكون . فهذا يقتضي أن يكون الإدراك حالة مغايرة لمجرد الحضور . وهو المطلوب · الثاني : وهو إن الخيال الذي هو خزانة الحس المشترك ، قد حصلت الصور الجسمانية عنده ، مع أن قوة الإدراك غير حاصلة ، وذلك يدل على قولنا . الثالث : إن أشباح المبصرات تنطبع في كل واحد من الجليدتين ، والإدراك غير حاصل هناك ، وإلا لزم أن يدرك الإنسان : المرئي الواحد شيئين . لأنه انطبع في كل واحد من الجليدتين صورة على حدة . بل الحق أن الإنطباع يحصل في الجليدتين . إلا أن قوة الإبصار إنما تحصل عند ملتقى العصبتين المجوفتين . هكذا قاله الشيخ في كتاب : الشفاء ، وهذا يدل على أن الإدراك غير الانطباع . الرابع : وهو أنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن الإدراك والشعور والإبصار : أحوال نسبية إضافية ، فيمتنع أن يكون عبارة عن مجرد حضور تلك الماهية . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : القوة العقلية حالة في جسم مخصوص ، ثم إن القوة الناطقة قد تحصل لها الحالة الإضافية المسماة بالشعور والإدراك ، فحينئذ تصير القوة العاقلة مدركة لتلك الآلة ، وقد لا توجد تلك الآلة الإضافية ، فتصير غافلة عن تلك الآلة ، وإذا كان هذا الإحتمال الظاهر القوي الحق قائما ، فقد سقطت هذه الشبهة بالكلية . والعجب من الشيخ كيف جوز عقله أن يسمى مثل هذا الكلام الركيك بالبرهان العظيم
And perception: one may say that perception is identical to that form; but no rational person says this. Evidence for this is in several aspects: First: a body described by blackness, blackness exists in it, but perception of blackness does not exist in it; so perception of blackness is different from the existence of the essence of blackness. If they say: the existence of the essence is only perception of it when that thing at which the essence is present is a perceiving power; so if the body is inanimate, this condition is absent, so the conditioned is absent. We say in response: if perception is merely the essence present at it, then the perceiver must be that thing at which the essence is present; therefore an inanimate object must be a perceiver. And if we say that not whenever the essence is present at it, but rather it may be a perceiver of it or not, then this implies that perception is a state distinct from mere presence, which is what is sought. Second: that the imagination, which is the treasury of common sense, has corporeal forms present at it, while the power of perception is not present; this supports our claim. Third: the images of visible objects are impressed in each of the two lenses, but perception is not present there; otherwise a person would perceive a single visible object as two things, because it is impressed in each lens as a separate image. Rather, the truth is that the impression occurs in the two lenses, but the power of sight only occurs at the meeting point of the two hollow nerves. Thus said the Master (al-Shaykh) in the book al-Shifa'; this indicates that perception is not the impression. Fourth: we know by necessity that perception, sensation, and sight are relative, relational states; therefore it is impossible that they are merely the presence of that essence. And if this is established, we say: why is it not permissible to say that the rational power is a state in a particular body, then the rational soul may acquire the relational state called sensation and perception, so then the rational power becomes perceiving of that instrument, and sometimes that relational instrument does not exist, so it becomes unaware of that instrument? And if this strong, true, apparent possibility stands, then this sophism is completely refuted. It is surprising how the Master's intellect permitted him to call such weak speech a great demonstration.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a series of proofs refuting the identity theory of perception (that perception is nothing but the presence of the form in the perceiver). The author presents four arguments: First, if perception were just the presence of the form, then an inanimate body like a black thing would perceive its own blackness, which is absurd. The opponent may object that a condition is needed (the perceiver must be a perceiving power), but the author responds that if perception is merely presence, then even without that condition, the thing is a perceiver; if the condition is needed, then perception must be something extra. Second, the imagination contains forms without actual perception. Third, images are impressed in the lenses of the eye, but perception occurs only at the optic chiasm, so perception is not the impression. Fourth, perception is a relative state, not a mere presence. The author then concludes that the identity theory is refuted and expresses surprise that the Master (Ibn Sina) would call such reasoning a great proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perception is identical to the mere presence of the form.
Correction: Perception is a relational state distinct from the form's presence; otherwise, inanimate bodies would perceive.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Perception is not the mere presence of the form but an additional relational state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that perception is not the mere presence of a form but an additional relational state. In your own life, when have you been physically present—hearing the Quran, sitting in prayer—but your heart was not truly perceiving? What would it take for you to turn that presence into real relational awareness? This lesson forces you to ask: are you merely present, or are you truly perceiving? Commit to one change today to bridge that gap.
?
The fourth proof says perception is inherently relational. Your knowledge of Allah—is it just a concept stored in your mind, or is it a living, relational awareness that transforms you? If it is only conceptual presence, then according to this lesson, it is not genuine perception. How will you cultivate that relational knowledge today?
?
The third proof shows that images in both eyes are not enough for sight; sight requires a meeting point. Your daily experiences flood your senses, but how often do they 'meet' in your heart and mind to produce true understanding? Reflect on one moment today where you can intentionally bring your scattered perceptions into a focused, relational awareness of Allah's signs.

The Second Objection: Mental Form vs. External Object
Author refutes the claim that the mental form equals the external object in all respects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mental form cannot be completely equal to the external object, and how this refutes the objection of two similars.

Core Idea
The mental form is not identical to the external object; claiming otherwise leads to absurdities.

This lesson addresses a key objection in the theory of perception: if mental forms must exactly mirror external objects, then thinking of God would require God's essence in the mind, which is impossible. The author presents three arguments to show that equality is false, and then turns the objection on its head.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without breaking it apart. Then read the English translation. Notice how the author structures the objection and then refutes it point by point.
2
Identify the three arguments
The passage lists three separate proofs that the mental form cannot equal the external object. Locate each one and note the absurd consequence in each case.
The arguments use examples of God, heaven/earth, and fire/snow.
3
Understand the conclusion
After the three arguments, the author draws the conclusion: since equality is false, the problem of two similars does not arise. Reflect on how this resolves the original objection.
The key move is that the objector's premise is false.
4
Review key terms
Ensure you understand the Arabic terms: المعقول (the intelligible), الصورة العقلية (mental form), المساواة (equality), اجتماع المثلين (meeting of two similars).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : أن يقال : أتدعون أنا إذا عقلنا شيئاً ، فإن الصورة الحاضرة في العقل مساوية لذلك المعقول من جميع الوجوه والاعتبارات، أو لا توجبون حصول هذه المساواة من كل الوجوه ؟ والأول لا يقوله عاقل . وتقريره من وجوه : الأول : إنا إذا عقلنا الله تعالى وجب أن تكون الصورة الحاصلة في عقولنا : موجوداً واجب الوجود لذاته ، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل الثاني : إنا إذا عقلنا السماء والأرض ، فهذه الصورة العقلية : كيفية قائمة بالنفس جزئية . وهذه الكيفية لا تحس ولا تلمس ، والعلم الضروري حاصل بأنها لا تساوي هذا الفلك في جميع أجزاء الماهية والصفات الثالث : إنه لو كان الأمر كذلك ، فإذا عقلنا النار ، وجب أن تحصل النار في عقولنا . وإذا عقلنا الثلج ، وجب أن يحصل الثلج في عقولنا ، فتكون النار العقلية ، والثلج العقلي، مساوياً للنار والثلج الموجودين في الخارج ، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل . وأما إذا سلمتم : أن الصورة العقلية ، لا يجب أن تكون مساوية لذات المعقول من جميع الوجوه ، فحينئذ لا يلزم من حدوث صورة أخرى في القلب ، أو الدماغ في القوة العاقلة : اجتماع المثلين
The second question: It may be said: Do you claim that when we conceive something, the form present in the intellect is equal to that intelligible in all respects and considerations, or do you not require this equality in all respects? The first (that they are equal) no rational person says. Its elaboration in several aspects: First: If we conceive God, then the form occurring in our intellects must be an existent necessary of existence by itself, which no rational person says. Second: If we conceive the heaven and the earth, this mental form is a quality subsisting in the soul, particular. This quality is not perceived by sight or touch, and necessary knowledge exists that it is not equal to this celestial sphere in all parts of essence and attributes. Third: If it were so, then when we conceive fire, fire must occur in our intellects; and when we conceive snow, snow must occur in our intellects; so the mental fire and mental snow would be equal to the fire and snow existing externally, which no rational person says. But if you concede that the mental form need not be equal to the essence of the intelligible in all respects, then it does not follow from the occurrence of another form in the heart or brain in the rational power that there is a meeting of two similars.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its refutation. The objector asks whether the mental form must be equal to the external object in all respects. The author states that no rational person claims complete equality, and gives three proofs: First, thinking of God would require God's essence to be present in the mind, which is absurd. Second, thinking of the heavens and earth produces a particular quality in the soul that is not sensible and cannot be equal to the vast physical universe. Third, thinking of fire or snow would entail having fire and snow in the mind, which is false. The author then concludes: since you concede inequality, the problem of two similars (when two forms of the same object occur) does not arise.

الصورة العقلية
المساواة
اجتماع المثلين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that mental forms must exactly mirror external objects for knowledge to be valid.
Correction: The passage shows that mental forms are not identical to external objects; they are representations that lack many attributes of the originals.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection about?
A) Perception is not a relation
B) The mental form must be equal to the object in all respects
C) Two forms cannot coexist
D) The soul is immaterial

Key Takeaway
The mental form does not need to be equal to the external object; therefore, multiple mental representations of the same object do not cause a logical problem.

Third Objection: The Distinction of Loci
Objection questioning why two similar forms cannot coexist if they are in different loci.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the impossibility of two similar forms and the reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
Similar forms might coexist if their loci are different.

In kalam, the impossibility of two similar forms coexisting in the same substrate is a key principle. But what if they are in different substrates? This objection challenges the principle by proposing a scenario where one form is in the rational power and another in its substrate, questioning why that is insufficient for distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the structure: the objector concedes the entailment but questions the impossibility.
The objection begins with 'Suppose that...' and then explains the reasoning.
2
Identify the key reasoning
The objector argues that the two forms are in different loci: one in the heart/brain, the other in the rational power that inheres there. This difference should prevent identity.
Pay attention to the terms 'محل' (locus) and 'مغايرة' (distinction).
3
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why this objection is powerful. If the distinction of loci is accepted, it undermines the argument against multiple similar forms. Ask yourself how a defender might respond.
The answer to this objection is not given in this lesson, so think critically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : هب أنه يلزم اجتماع المثلين . فلم قلتم : إن ذلك محال ؟ وبيانه : أن القوة العاقلة حالة في جوهر القلب أو الدماغ ، وهذه الصورة الحادثة : حالة في القوة العاقلة ، فإحدى الصورتين حالة محل القوة العاقلة ، والثانية حالة فيها . فلم لا يكفي هذا القدر من التفاوت في حصول المغايرة ؟
The third question: Suppose that a meeting of two similar forms is entailed. Why do you say that it is impossible? Its explanation: that the rational power is a state in the substance of the heart or brain, and this occurring form is a state in the rational power; so one of the two forms is a state of the locus of the rational power, and the second is a state in it. So why is this degree of difference not sufficient for obtaining distinction?
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection against the impossibility of two similar forms coexisting. The objector concedes that the argument might entail the meeting of two similar forms, but questions why that is considered impossible. He provides an explanation: the rational power inheres in the heart or brain, and the new form inheres in that same rational power. Thus one form is in the substrate (the heart/brain) and the other is in the power that inheres in that substrate. This difference in locus (one in the substrate, one in the power) should be sufficient to avoid identity. The objector demands a reason why this distinction is not enough. The answer to this objection is not provided in the given window.

Key Terms

القوة العاقلة
المحل
المغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two similar forms can never coexist in any way.
Correction: This objection argues that they might coexist if they are in different loci, such as the substrate and the power in it. The impossibility is not absolute but depends on the locus.

?
If the objection is correct, two similar forms can coexist if they are in different loci. In your life, you may hold two similar beliefs (e.g., about trust and caution) that seem to contradict each other but might actually belong to different domains (e.g., personal relationships vs. professional decisions). Are there areas where you accuse yourself of inconsistency without considering different 'loci'? Examine one such area today and decide if the difference in context justifies coexistence.

Proof of an Immaterial Locus for Mental Images
Author proves that mental images require an immaterial substance as their locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that mental images, such as the image of a mountain, cannot reside in the physical brain and therefore require an immaterial substance (the soul) as their locus.

Core Idea
Mental images too large for the brain prove the soul is immaterial.

This lesson presents a concise yet powerful argument from the nature of mental images. The author shows that since we have images of huge objects (like the sea) and we know they are not physically present, they must exist in the mind. But where in the mind? The brain is too small to contain such images. Therefore, the locus must be an immaterial substance—the soul. This argument relies on the axiom that a large image cannot be impressed into a small physical space.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: existence of images → not external → mind → locus options → rejection → conclusion.
The argument is presented as a formal proof (dalīl).
2
Identify the three possible loci
The author lists three possibilities for where mental images reside: a body, in a body, or neither. Locate these in the text and note how they are presented.
The first two are 'jism' and 'ḥāl fī al-jism'.
3
Understand why the first two are rejected
The reason given is the size disparity: images like the sea are immense, but the brain is small. Impression of large in small is irrational. Grasp this key point.
This relies on 'badīhat al-ʿaql'—axiom of reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت: أن هذه الصور الخيالية: موجودة . ولما علمنا بالضرورة : أنها ليست موجودة في الأعيان ، ثبت أنها موجودة في الأذهان . فتقول : محل هذه الصورة، إما أن يكون جسماً ، أو حالا في الجسم ، أو لا جسم ولا حالاً في الجسم . والقسمان الأولان باطلان . لأن صورة البحر والجبل والشموس والأقمار : صوراً عظيمة ، والدماغ : جسم صغير . وانطباع الصور العظيمة في المحل الصغير : محال في بديهة العقل . ولما بطل ذلك ، ثبت : أن محل هذه الصور الخيالية موجودة ، ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني . وذلك هو المطلوب ، هذا تقرير هذا الدليل
Thus it is established that these mental images (al-ṣuwar al-khayāliyya) exist. And since we know by necessity that they do not exist in external objects, it is established that they exist in minds. So we say: the locus (maḥall) of this image is either a body, or in a body, or neither a body nor in a body. The first two divisions are invalid. Because the image of the sea, the mountain, the suns, and the moons are immense images, and the brain is a small body. And the impression (inṭibāʿ) of immense images in a small locus is impossible by the axiom of reason (badīhat al-ʿaql). And when that is invalidated, it is established that the locus of these mental images exists, not being a body nor corporeal (jismānī). And that is the desired conclusion. This is the exposition of this proof.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (dalīl) for the existence of an immaterial substance, namely the soul or intellect, as the locus of mental images. The argument proceeds in several steps:

1. **Existence of mental images**: The author asserts that mental images exist, based on our ability to perceive and distinguish them. They are not pure nothingness because they have effects in our cognition.

2. **Not in external objects**: We know intuitively that these images are not present in the external world; for example, the image of a mountain is not actually in the physical mountain.

3. **So they exist in minds**: Since they exist and are not external, they must exist in the mind (al-adhhān).

4. **Determining the locus**: The locus of these images must be either (a) a body, (b) something in a body, or (c) neither a body nor something in a body. The first two options are rejected because the brain (a small body) cannot contain huge images like the sea or mountains; that would be impossible by the axiom of reason.

5. **Conclusion**: Therefore, the locus must be of category (c) – something that is neither a body nor corporeal. This is the immaterial substance (jawhar mujarrad) that is the soul. The proof ends by stating that this is what was sought.

The author presents this as a lighter argument compared to earlier proofs, relying on the premise that perception requires impression (inṭibāʿ) in the locus.

Key Terms
الصور الخيالية (al-ṣuwar al-khayāliyya)
المحل (al-maḥall)
الانطباع (al-inṭibāʿ)
بديهة العقل (badīhat al-ʿaql)
الجسماني (al-jismānī)
الجوهر المجرد (al-jawhar al-mujarrad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental images are located somewhere in the physical brain, like a picture stored in a small space.
Correction: The author argues that immense images cannot physically fit into a small brain; thus, they cannot be in a body or in a body. This forces the conclusion that their locus is immaterial.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the first two options for the locus of mental images are rejected?
A) Because the brain is too small to contain large images
B) Because mental images are not real
C) Because the brain is immaterial
D) Because mental images exist in external objects

Key Takeaway
Mental images cannot be physically located in the brain; their immaterial locus proves the existence of an immaterial substance (the soul).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine the vastness of the universe, your mind contains that image. If your brain cannot physically hold such an image, where is it actually? Can you avoid the conclusion that something immaterial within you is the container? How does that change how you view yourself?

The author says the impossibility is known by 'badīhat al-ʿaql'—the axiom of reason. When do you suppress your own reasoning about the immaterial aspects of your existence because they are uncomfortable? Are there assumptions you hold about yourself that this proof challenges?

If the locus of your mental images is immaterial, and that immaterial substance is your soul, what does that imply about the permanence of your thoughts and memories? Do you live as if you have an immortal soul, or as if you are merely a brain that will decay? What would change if you truly believed this proof?

The Impossibility of Mental Images in an Immaterial Substance: The Two Squares Argument
Proof by contradiction using two mental squares.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that mental images cannot exist in a purely immaterial substance, using the example of two squares in the mind.

Core Idea
Distinct mental images require distinct loci; only divisible bodies can host multiple loci.

This lesson presents a key argument from philosophical theology: the impossibility of multiple mental images inhering in a single immaterial substance. The author constructs a reductio ad absurdum using two imagined squares. By examining the conditions for distinction, we will see why the images' loci must differ, and why only a bodily substance can satisfy that requirement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument's structure.
Note the logical flow: premises, two possibilities, refutation, conclusion.
2
Identify the key premises
Notice that the argument begins with two distinct squares existing in the mind. Then it considers the locus (mahall) of each: either the same or different.
The term 'mahall' refers to the substratum in which the image inheres.
3
Analyze the 'same locus' scenario
The author shows that if both squares share one locus, they cannot be distinguished. Distinction does not come from essence (both are squares) nor from accidents (since any accident would affect both equally).
This is the core of the reductio: the assumption leads to an absurd conclusion.
4
Conclude the necessity of distinct loci
Since we do distinguish the two squares, their loci must be different. But for two distinct loci to exist within a single substance, that substance must be divisible – it must be a body.
Thus, mental images cannot inhere in an indivisible, immaterial substance.
5
Review the conclusion and significance
The proof concludes that the occurrence of mental images in an immaterial substance is impossible (muhal). Reflect on how this argument fits into broader theological discussions about the soul and intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل واحد منهما عن الآخر فهذان المربعان لا بد وأن يكونا موجودين ، وإذا كانا لا موجودين في الأعيان ، فهما موجودان في الأذهان. فإما أن يكون محل كل واحد منهما عين الآخر ، أو غيره . فإن كان الأول امتنع امتياز أحدهما عن الآخر . لأن الإمتياز ليس بالماهية ولوازمها . لأن الماهية واحدة . ولا بالعوارض لأنهما لما حلا في محل واحد . فكل ما يفرض عارضاً لأحدهما ، كانت نسبة ذلك العارض إلى أحدهما كنسبته إلى الثاني . وإذا صار العارض مشتركاً فيه ، امتنع بقاء الإمتياز ، وإذا لم يبق الامتياز في الذهب [؟] ، وجب أن لا يميز الخيال أحد ذينك المربعين عن الآخر ، وحيث حصل هذا الإمتياز وجب أن يكون محل إحدى الصورتين مغايراً لمحل الصورة الأخرى ، وذلك لا يحصل إلا إذا كان المحل جسماً . فثبت : أن حصول هذه الصورة الخيالية في الجوهر المجرد : محال

فهذا تمام الكلام على هذه الحجة

وههنا آخر الكلام في الاعتراض على هذه الدلائل ، التي زعموا أنها من الدلائل اليقينية القطعية والله أعلم
Each one of them from the other. So these two squares must exist, and if they do not exist in the external world, then they exist in the minds. Then the locus of each one of them is either identical to the other or different. If the first is the case, then the distinction of one from the other is impossible. Because distinction is not by the essence and its necessary concomitants—since the essence is one—nor by the accidents, because when they both occupy one locus, every accident supposed for one of them has the same relation to the one as to the second. And when the accident becomes shared in it, the continuation of distinction is impossible. And if distinction does not remain in the mind, then the imagination must not distinguish one of those two squares from the other. But when this distinction does occur, the locus of one of the images must be different from the locus of the other image. And that does not happen unless the locus is a body. So it is established: that the occurrence of this mental image in the immaterial substance is impossible.

This is the complete discussion on this proof.

And here ends the speech on objecting to these proofs, which they claimed are among the certain definitive proofs. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a proof by refutation: the author demonstrates the impossibility of mental images (suwar khayaliyya) subsisting in a purely immaterial substance (jawhar mujarrad). The argument proceeds as follows: Take two distinct mental images, such as two squares. These exist in the mind (al-adhhan). Now consider their locus (mahall) — that in which they inhere. The locus of each is either identical to the other or different. If they share one and the same locus, then they cannot be distinguished from each other. Why? Because distinction does not arise from the essence (mahiyya) and its necessary concomitants, since the essence of the two images as squares is one. Nor can distinction arise from accidents (awarid), because if both images reside in the same locus, any accident attributed to one would equally attach to the other, making the accident shared and thus incapable of differentiating them. Consequently, if the locus were one, the imagination could not tell the two squares apart. But we do distinguish them — therefore their loci must be different. For two different loci to exist within a single substance, that substance must be divisible; only a body (jism) can have multiple distinct loci. Hence a purely immaterial substance, which is indivisible, cannot host multiple distinct mental images. The author concludes that the occurrence of a mental image in an immaterial substance is impossible (muhal). The following lines close the discussion and note that this concludes the objections against those particular proofs that some claimed to be certain.

Key Terms
صورة خيالية
جوهر مجرد
محل
امتياز
عارض
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this argument proves that the human soul is corporeal, since mental images occur in the soul.
Correction: This argument only targets the possibility of multiple distinct mental images in a single immaterial substance. It does not directly address the nature of the soul itself; it simply shows that if mental images are distinct, their locus must be divisible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can two distinct mental images not share a single locus?
A) Because their essences differ.
B) Because the locus would then be divisible and thus not strictly one.
C) Because any accident would apply equally to both images, removing the basis for distinction.
D) Because the imagination cannot perceive two images at once.

Key Takeaway
Two distinct mental images cannot subsist in a single immaterial substance because they would become indistinguishable; hence such images require a divisible locus, i.e., a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine two different scenes simultaneously, do you experience them as separated in your mind? What does this argument imply about the nature of your own self? Are you willing to accept that your mind might be divisible, like a body, or does this force you to reconsider what you mean by 'my mind'?
?
The proof shows that two distinct images cannot share the same locus. Reflect on a time you held two conflicting ideas in your head at once. Did they remain distinct, or did they blur? According to this lesson, what must be true about the 'place' where those ideas reside? How does that challenge your view of your own identity?

The First Proof: The Rational Soul is Not Corporeal
Avicenna presents the first proof that the rational soul is not corporeal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of Avicenna's first conditional proof for the immateriality of the rational soul and how it defends against a common objection.

Core Idea
If the soul were corporeal, it would weaken in old age—but it doesn't, so it's not.

In this passage, Avicenna presents a classic argument from the ancient philosophers: a conditional syllogism that aims to demonstrate the rational soul's immateriality. The argument hinges on the observation that many elderly people retain their mental faculties despite bodily decline. Let's examine each step of this proof and the objection it anticipates.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical form
The proof is a conditional syllogism. Identify the antecedent (if the rational power is corporeal) and the consequent (it would weaken constantly in old age). The argument then denies the consequent to conclude the antecedent is false.
This is a classic modus tollens argument.
2
Examine the explanation of the conditional
The text explains why the conditional is true: because the temperament deteriorates in old age, and any bodily power depends on the temperament. Therefore, a corporeal power would necessarily weaken.
3
Examine the denial of the consequent
The text argues that the consequent is false because many old people do not experience deterioration of their rational power. This observation is key to the proof.
Note the word 'dāʾiman' (constantly) – the proof does not require that no one ever loses intellect, only that it is not a universal constant.
4
Consider the objection and its refutation
An objector says some elderly become senile, which would support the conditional. The author claims this objection is refuted upon proper investigation, though the full refutation is not given here.
This sets up a need for a more detailed response, perhaps in later passages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس في حكاية الدلائل الاقناعية التي خكروها في أن النفس الناطقة موردة من الجسية ذكر الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي وجوهاً كثيرة في هذا الباب، نقلها عن قدماء الفلاسفة. الحجة الأولى: قالوا: لو كانت القوة العقلية جسدانية، لضعفت في زمان الشيخوخة دائماً ولكنها لم تضعف في زمان الشيخوخة دائماً فهي غير جسدانية. بيان الشرطية: إن المزاج يختل في زمان الشيخوخة. ومتى ضعف المحتاج إليه، فإنه يلزم منه ضعف المحتاج. فلو كانت القوة العقلية بدنية، لزم من اختلال البدن، اختلالها. لكن البدن يختل عند الشيخوخة، فكان يلزم لا محالة اختلال القوة العقلية عند الشيخوخة. وأما بيان عدم التالي: فلأن كثيراً من الشيوخ لا تختل قوتهم العقلية البتة. فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن الشرطية حقة، وثبت أن التالي مفقود، فوجب كون المقدم كاذباً. ثم قال هذا المستدل: وليس للسائل أن يقول: إن الشيخ إذا توغل في الشيخوخة، فإنه يصير خرفاً ويزول عقله. وهذا يدل على أن القوة العقلية جسدانية. قال: وذلك لأن هذا السؤال مدفوع، نظراً إلى البحث.
Chapter Five: On relating the persuasive arguments that they mentioned concerning that the rational soul is derived from the body. The Chief Master (Abu Ali) mentioned many aspects in this chapter, transmitted from the ancient philosophers. The first proof: They said: If the rational power were corporeal, it would weaken constantly in old age. But it does not weaken constantly in old age, so it is not corporeal. Explanation of the conditional: The temperament deteriorates in old age. When that which is needed weakens, then the needful thing necessarily weakens. So if the rational power were bodily, then from the deterioration of the body, its deterioration would follow. But the body deteriorates at old age, so there would necessarily follow the deterioration of the rational power at old age. As for the explanation of the denial of the consequent: because many old people do not have their rational power deteriorate at all. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the conditional is true, and it is established that the consequent is absent, so it is necessary that the antecedent be false. Then this arguer said: It is not for the questioner to say: When the old person becomes advanced in old age, he becomes senile and his intellect leaves him. And this indicates that the rational power is corporeal. He said: That is because this objection is refuted, given [proper] investigation.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-ḥujja al-ūlā) for the immateriality of the rational soul, as transmitted from the ancient philosophers by Avicenna. The proof is a conditional syllogism (sharṭiyya). It begins with the conditional premise: 'If the rational power (al-quwwa al-ʿaqliyya) were corporeal (jasmāniyya), it would weaken constantly in old age.' Then it observes that this consequent does not obtain: 'But it does not weaken constantly in old age.' Therefore the antecedent is false: 'So it is not corporeal.' The author then explains the conditional (bayān al-sharṭiyya): the temperament (al-mizāj) deteriorates in old age, and when that on which something depends weakens, the dependent thing weakens. So if the rational power were bodily (badaniyya), the deterioration of the body would necessitate its deterioration. But the body does deteriorate, so the rational power would have to deteriorate. Then the denial of the consequent (bayān ʿadam al-tālī) is explained: many old people do not experience any deterioration of their rational power. Thus the conditional holds, the consequent is false, so the antecedent must be false. Finally, an objection is addressed: the objector says that some elderly do become senile and lose their intellect, which would imply the rational power is corporeal. The author replies that this objection is refuted (madfūʿ) upon proper investigation (naẓaran ilā al-baḥth). The passage does not elaborate the refutation, but it sets up the need for a more detailed response.

Key Terms

جسدانية
الشيخوخة
المزاج
بدنية
الشرطية
المقدم
التالى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof fails because some elderly become senile, showing the rational power does weaken.
Correction: The proof only requires that the rational power does not weaken constantly (dāʾiman) in old age. The existence of some senile individuals does not disprove the claim that many elderly retain their intellect, which is sufficient to deny the universal conditional.

Checkpoint
What is the conditional premise in the first proof?
A) If the rational power were corporeal, it would remain strong in old age.
B) If the rational power were corporeal, it would weaken constantly in old age.
C) If the rational power were corporeal, it would be affected by temperament.
D) If the rational power were corporeal, it would depend on the body.

Key Takeaway
Avicenna's first proof uses a conditional syllogism to argue that the rational soul cannot be corporeal because it does not inevitably weaken with the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body will inevitably weaken and decay. What are you actively doing today to strengthen your rational soul so that it remains sharp when your body fails?
?
If you know the soul is not corporeal, why do you still invest most of your energy into physical pleasures and comforts rather than into knowledge and spiritual growth?
?
When you encounter an old person with a sharp mind, does it challenge your assumption that aging is all decline? What does this tell you about the nature of the soul and your own responsibility to nurture it?

First Proof for the Soul's Incorporeality
Avicenna presents the first proof for the soul's incorporeality via a conditional syllogism and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first proof for the soul's incorporeality, including its conditional syllogism and the author's refutation of a common objection.

Core Idea
The soul cannot be corporeal because its intellectual powers do not necessarily decline with age.

Avicenna presents a concise yet powerful argument for the incorporeality of the rational soul. The proof uses a conditional reasoning: if the soul were dependent on the body, it would inevitably weaken as the body ages. Since many elderly people retain full mental faculties, the soul cannot be bodily. This simple form hides a sophisticated logical and rational defense against objections. In this lesson, we walk through the argument step by step, examining its logical structure and the author's response to a potential counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof's structure
Recognize that the proof is a conditional syllogism in the form of modus tollens: If the rational soul were corporeal, then it would always weaken in old age; but it does not always weaken; therefore it is not corporeal. Note the key terms: الشرطية (conditional), اللازم (consequent), الملزوم (antecedent).
2
Examine the justification of the conditional
The author explains why the conditional is true: the body's temperament declines in old age, and if the soul depends on the body, its weakness must follow from bodily weakness. This is the 'explanation of the conditional' (بيان الشرطية).
The argument assumes the soul's reliance on the body for its operations if it were corporeal.
3
Examine the denial of the consequent
The author shows that the consequent (always weakening) is false: many old people do not experience mental decline. This is the 'demonstration of the absence of the consequent' (بيان عدم التالي). Thus the antecedent (being corporeal) must be false.
4
Analyze the objection and refutation
A potential objection is raised: some old people become senile, which might support the soul's corporeality. The author refutes it from two angles: logical (the objection commits the fallacy of 'exception from the consequent') and rational (the occasional co-occurrence does not prove necessary dependence, and could be due to the soul's preoccupation with the body).
The refutation shows that the proof remains valid despite counterexamples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس في حكاية الدلائل الاقناعية التي ذكروها في أن النفس الناطقة مبردة من الجسدية. ذكر الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي وجوهاً كثيرة في هذا الباب، نقلها عن قدماء الفلاسفة. الحجة الأولى: قالوا: لو كانت القوة العقلية جسدانية، لضعفت في زمان الشيخوخة دائماً ولكنها لم تضعف في زمان الشيخوخة دائماً فهي غير جسدانية. بيان الشرطية: إن المزاج يختل في زمان الشيخوخة. ومتى ضعف المحتاج إليه، فإنه يلزم منه ضعف المحتاج. فلو كانت القوة العقلية بدنية، لزم من اختلال البدن، اختلالها. لكن البدن يختل عند الشيخوخة، فكان يلزم لا محالة اختلال القوة العقلية عند الشيخوخة. وأما بيان عدم التالي: فلان كثيراً من الشيوخ لا تختل قوتهم العقلية البتة. فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن الشرطية حقة، وثبت أن التالي مفقود، فوجب كون المقدم كاذباً. ثم قال هذا المستدل: وليس للسائل أن يقول: إن الشيخ إذا توغل في الشيخوخة، فإنه يصير خرفاً ويزول عقله. وهذا يدل على أن القوة العقلية جسدانية. قال: وذلك لأن هذا السؤال مدفوع، نظراً إلى البحث المنطقي، ونظراً إلى البحث العقلي. أما البحث المنطقي فهو أنا قلنا: إن كانت القوة العقلية بدنية، لزم من حصول الخلل في البدن، حصول الخلل في القوة العقلية. وهذا اللزوم غير حاصل، لأنه قد يبقى الشيخ مع كمال العقل، فوجب أن لا تكون القوة العقلية بدنية، لأن انتفاء اللازم يدل على انتفاء الملزوم. أما إذا قلنا: إن هذا الخلل قد يحصل مع الشيخوخة، فإنه لا ينتج شيئاً لأن الاستثناء من التالي، لا ينتج شيئاً. وأما البحث العقلي؛ فهو بينا أنه لو كانت القوة العقلية بدنية، لوجب عند حصول الخلل في البدن، أن يحصل الخلل في القوة العقلية، وأن لا ينفك ضعف البدن عن ضعف العقل. وحيث حصل هذا الانفكاك علمنا: أن القوة العقلية غنية في ذاتها عن البدن. جرم لم يلزم من حصول الخلل في البدن، حصول الخلل في العقل. وأما حصول الخلل في العقل في بعض أوقات حصول الخلل في البدن، فهذا لا يدل على كون القوة العقلية بدنية، لاحتمال أن يكون الموجب لاختلال العقل في هذه الصورة سبباً آخر وهو أن شدة اهتمام النفس بإصلاح البدن، يمنعها عن استكمال القوة النظرية. فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذا الدليل.
The fifth chapter on the narration of the persuasive proofs they mentioned that the rational soul is separate from the body. The chief master Abu Ali mentioned many aspects in this chapter, transmitted from the ancient philosophers. The first proof: They said: If the rational faculty were corporeal, it would necessarily weaken in old age always, but it does not weaken in old age always, so it is not corporeal. Explanation of the conditional: The temperament becomes imbalanced in old age. And whenever what is needed weakens, then the weakening of what needs it necessarily follows. So if the rational faculty were bodily, then from the imbalance of the body, its imbalance would necessarily follow. But the body becomes imbalanced in old age, so the imbalance of the rational faculty in old age would necessarily follow. As for the demonstration of the absence of the consequent: because many old people's rational faculty does not become imbalanced at all. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the conditional is true, and it is established that the consequent is absent, so the antecedent must be false. Then this arguer said: The questioner cannot say that when the old man becomes very old, he becomes senile and his mind goes, and this indicates that the rational faculty is corporeal. He said: Because this question is refuted, from the perspective of logical inquiry and from the perspective of rational inquiry. As for the logical inquiry: we said: If the rational faculty were bodily, then from the occurrence of defect in the body, the occurrence of defect in the rational faculty would necessarily follow. But this necessity does not occur, because the old man may remain with full intellect, so the rational faculty must not be bodily, because the negation of the necessary indicates the negation of the necessitated. As for when we say that this defect may occur with old age, it does not produce anything, because the exception from the consequent does not produce anything. As for the rational inquiry: we demonstrated that if the rational faculty were bodily, then at the occurrence of defect in the body, the defect in the rational faculty must occur, and the weakness of the body cannot be separated from the weakness of the intellect. But since this separation occurred, we know that the rational faculty is independent in its essence from the body. Therefore, the occurrence of defect in the body does not necessitate the occurrence of defect in the intellect. As for the occurrence of defect in the intellect at some times of defect in the body, this does not indicate that the rational faculty is bodily, because it is possible that the cause of the disturbance of the intellect in this case is another reason, namely that the soul's intense concern with repairing the body prevents it from perfecting the theoretical faculty. So this completes the discussion in establishing this proof.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the incorporeality of the rational soul, attributed to ancient philosophers and transmitted by Avicenna (الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي). The proof is a conditional syllogism: If the rational faculty were corporeal, it would always weaken in old age; but it does not always weaken; therefore it is not corporeal. The author then explains the conditional statement (بيان الشرطية) by noting that the body's temperament deteriorates with age, and if a thing is dependent on the body, its weakness follows from the body's weakness. The negation of the consequent (بيان عدم التالي) is shown by the fact that many elderly people retain full mental capacity. Thus the antecedent (that it is bodily) must be false. A potential objection is raised: some elderly people become senile, which might suggest a bodily dependence. The author refutes this from two angles: logical and rational. Logically, the objection attempts to argue from the existence of defect in some cases, but that does not prove a necessary connection; the argument requires a universal conditional. Rationally, if the rational faculty were bodily, it could never be separated from bodily weakness; but we observe cases where the body is weak but the intellect is strong, proving independence. The occasional co-occurrence of mental and bodily decline may be due to the soul's preoccupation with fixing the body, not a direct dependence. The passage concludes by stating that this completes the establishment of the proof.

اللازم
الملزوم

الاستثناء من التالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is invalid because some elderly people do lose their mental faculties.
Correction: The proof does not require that no elderly person ever loses mental faculties; it only requires that the loss is not a necessary consequence of old age. The observation that many elderly retain full mental capacity disproves the necessary connection, so the argument remains valid.

Checkpoint
What is the logical structure of the first proof for the soul's incorporeality?
A) Modus ponens: if bodily, then weak; weak, so bodily.
B) Modus tollens: if bodily, then always weak; not always weak, so not bodily.
C) Disjunctive syllogism: either bodily or not; not not bodily, so bodily.
D) Causal inference: bodily causes weakness, weakness absent, so not bodily.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for the soul's incorporeality uses a modus tollens argument: if the soul were corporeal, it would always weaken in old age; but it does not always weaken, so it is not corporeal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your rational soul is not dependent on your aging body, what excuse do you have for neglecting intellectual and spiritual growth as you grow older? How will you ensure your soul remains active and seeking truth despite bodily weaknesses?
?
The passage suggests that mental decline in some elderly may be because the soul is too preoccupied with repairing the body. Are you allowing the concerns of your body—health, comfort, appearance—to dominate your soul's pursuit of higher understanding? What concrete step can you take today to reorient your focus?

Objection to the First Proof: The First Point
Objection to the first proof, first point explained.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the objection to the first proof and the first point about the minimal bodily health required for the rational faculty.

Core Idea
The objection targets the conditional link, not the conclusion.

In this passage, the author presents an objection that challenges the central assumption of the first proof. The objector denies that any bodily defect necessarily impairs the rational faculty, and begins to explain this from multiple angles. The first point introduces the idea of a minimal required health, arguing that perfect bodily health is not necessary. Note that the passage is cut off mid-sentence, so the argument is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall sense. Notice that the passage ends abruptly with 'it is not unlikely that it be said that...'.
The argument is incomplete; the text cuts off at the end of the page.
2
Identify the logical structure of the objection
The objector begins with 'We do not concede' (لا نسلّم) which denies the conditional premise of the proof. This is a direct attack on the logical link between bodily nature and impairment.
The objection is not simply rejecting the conclusion but questioning the assumed necessity.
3
Examine the first point: the concept of a specific required amount
The objector argues that only a specific amount of bodily health is necessary for the rational faculty to be perfect, not perfect health itself. Thus, a defect in the body does not necessarily impair the faculty if the minimal requirement is met.
This undermines the proof's assumption that any defect causes impairment.
4
Consider the significance of the objection
Ask yourself why this objection matters. If correct, it would show that the first proof's conditional premise is not universally true, thereby weakening or defeating that proof.
The objection is part of a larger dialectical exchange.

والاعتراض عليه: لا نسلّم إنه لو كانت القوة العقلية بدنية، لزم اختلالها بسبب حصول كل خلل في البدن. وبيانه من وجوه: الأول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: القدر المحتاج إليه من صحة البدن في كمال القوة العقلية: مقدار معين. وأما كمال حال البدن في الصحة فإنه غير معتبر في كمال حال القوة العقلية. وإذا كان هذا محتملا لم يبعد أن يقال: إن ذلك
And the objection to it: We do not concede that if the rational faculty were bodily, its impairment would necessarily follow due to every defect in the body. Its explanation is from several aspects: The first: Why is it not permissible to say: The amount needed from the health of the body for the perfection of the rational faculty is a specific amount. As for the perfection of the state of the body in health, it is not considered in the perfection of the state of the rational faculty. And if this is possible, it is not unlikely that it be said that...
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage introduces an objection to the first proof. The objector denies the central conditional premise: that if the rational faculty were bodily, any bodily defect would necessarily impair it. The objector promises to explain this from several aspects, but only the first is given here. The first point is that only a certain minimal level of bodily health is required for the rational faculty to function; complete bodily health is not necessary. Since this is a possibility, the objector argues, the proof's conditional is not necessarily true. The passage ends abruptly with 'it is not unlikely that it be said that...', leaving the argument unfinished.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is simply that the conclusion (that the rational faculty is not bodily) is false.
Correction: The objection actually denies the conditional premise that if it were bodily, any defect would impair it. It allows that the rational faculty could be bodily but still function with only minimal health.

Checkpoint

A) The rational faculty is not affected by the body at all.

Key Takeaway
The first point of the objection introduces the idea that only a minimal level of bodily health is required for the rational faculty, challenging the assumption that any defect would cause impairment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Eight Defenses for Intellectual Perception in Old Age
Eight enumerated points defending intellectual continuity in old age.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eight arguments used to defend intellectual perception in old age and why they are ultimately insufficient as proof for the soul's immateriality.

Core Idea
Eight points defend the intellect's endurance in old age, but they only weaken the case for immateriality.

The author presents eight points that respond to an objection against the first argument for the soul's immateriality. These points attempt to show that intellectual perception can remain strong despite bodily decline. However, after listing them, the author admits that they make the proof obscure and conjectural, and that such a weighty doctrine cannot rest on weak evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the flow of the eight points come through.
Notice that each point is introduced by a number (الثاني, الثالث, etc.).
2
Identify each of the eight points
Go through each point one by one. Note how each one reasons that intellectual perception persists despite bodily decline.
Some points are empirical, some are based on hadith, and some are hypothetical.
3
Analyze the author's conclusion
Read the concluding lines: 'even if one can force answers... the proof becomes obscure and conjectural.' Consider why the author undermines his own list.
This reveals the author's critical approach to his own arguments.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Ask yourself: what does this passage teach about the standards of proof in kalam? The weakness of these points shows the high bar for proving immateriality.
This connects to the learning objective.

القدر المحتاج إليه باقي إلى آخر الشيخوخة فلا جرم بقي العقل كاملاً إلى آخر الشيخوخة . الثاني : إن الشيخ لعله إنما يمكنه أن يستمر في الإدراكات العقلية على الصحة والكمال ، لأن عقله يتم بعضو من البدن ، يتأخر الفساد والاستحالة إليه ، وإن ظهرت الآفة في سائر القوى والأفعال . الثالث : إنه لا يمتنع أن تكون بعض الأمزجة أوفق لبعض القوى ، فلعل مزاج الشيخ أوفق للقوة العقلية . ولهذا السبب تقوى فيه القوى العقلية . الرابع : إن المزاج إذا كان في غاية القوة والشدة ، كانت سائر القوى قوية فتكون القوى الشهوانية والغضبية قوية جداً ، وقوة هذه القوى ، تمنع العقل من الاستكمال . فإذا حصلت الشيخوخة ، وحصل الضعف ، حصل بسبب الضعف ، ضعف في العقل ، وضعف في هذه القوى المانعة للعقل من الاستكمال ، ويقدر ما حصل من الضعف في العقل ، حصل الضعف في أضداد العقل ، فينجير النقصان من أحد الجانبين ، بالنقصان الحاصل في المواضع ، فيحصل الاعتدال . الخامس : إن الشيخ حفظ المقدمات الكثيرة ، ومارسها ، وكثرت تجاربه ، وهذه الأحوال تعينه على جودة الفكر وقوة النظر . فالنقصان الحاصل بسبب اختلال البدن ، صار مجبوراً بما ذكرنا . السادس : إن كثرة الأفعال سبب الحصول الملكات الراسخة ، فصارت الزيادة الحاصلة بهذا الطريق ، جابرة للنقصان الحاصل بسبب اختلال البدن . السابع : قال عليه السلام : يهرم ابن آدم ، ويشيب منه اثنان : الحرص والأمل ، والتجارب دالة على صدق هذه القضية ، مع أن الحرص والأمل من القوى الجسمانية والصفات الخيالية . ثم إن ضعف البدن ، لم يوجب ضعف هذه الصفات . فثبت : أنه لا يلزم من اختلال البدن وضعفه ، ضعف الصفات البدنية . الثامن : إن هذه الحجة لا تدل على كون النفس جوهراً مجرداً ، لأن بتقدير أن تكون النفس عبارة عن أجسام مشرقة صافية سماوية مخالفة للطبائع العنصرية ، وأنها لا تقبل الانحلال والذبول والتبدل . فحينئذ لا يلزم من حصول الضعف والانحلال في البدن حصولهما في جوهر النفس . واعلم : أن هذه الوجوه ، وإن كان يمكن تكلف الأجوبة عنها إلا أن الحجة مع هذه السؤالات والجوابات تصير خفية ظنية . والقول بأن الإنسان جوهر مجرد ، لا داخل العالم ولا خارجه : قول مهيب هائل . وإثبات مثل ذلك المذهب المهيب ، بمثل هذه الحجة الضعيفة الظنية : بعيد
The necessary capacity remains until the end of old age, so inevitably the intellect remains complete until the end of old age. Second: Perhaps the old man can only continue in intellectual perceptions with soundness and perfection because his intellect is completed by an organ of the body whose corruption and decay are delayed, even if affliction appears in the other powers and actions. Third: It is not impossible that some temperaments are more suitable for some powers; perhaps the old man's temperament is more suitable for the intellectual power. For this reason the intellectual powers become strong in him. Fourth: If the temperament is at the extreme of strength and intensity, the other powers become strong, so the appetitive and irascible powers become very strong, and the strength of these powers prevents the intellect from reaching perfection. When old age occurs and weakness occurs, due to the weakness, weakness occurs in the intellect, and weakness occurs in these powers that prevent the intellect from perfection, and to the extent that weakness has occurred in the intellect, weakness occurs in the opposites of the intellect, so deficiency is balanced from one side by the deficiency occurring in the places, so equilibrium results. Fifth: The old man has memorized many premises, practiced them, and his experiences have multiplied, and these states help him in the quality of thought and the strength of reflection. So the deficiency that occurs due to the disorder of the body becomes compensated by what we have mentioned. Sixth: The multiplicity of actions causes the acquisition of firmly rooted dispositions, so the increase obtained through this way becomes a compensator for the deficiency occurring due to the disorder of the body. Seventh: He (peace be upon him) said: 'The son of Adam ages, and two things in him remain young: covetousness and hope,' and experiences indicate the truth of this statement, even though covetousness and hope are among the corporeal powers and imaginary attributes. Then the weakness of the body did not cause weakness in these attributes. So it is established that weakness of bodily attributes is not necessarily entailed by the disorder and weakness of the body. Eighth: This proof does not indicate that the soul is an immaterial substance, because if we suppose that the soul consists of bright, pure, celestial bodies opposed to elemental natures, and that it does not accept dissolution, fading, or change, then it is not necessary from the occurrence of weakness and dissolution in the body that they occur in the substance of the soul. Know that these points, even if one can force answers to them, the proof with these questions and answers becomes obscure and conjectural. And the statement that man is an immaterial substance, neither inside the world nor outside it, is a majestic and formidable statement. Proving such a majestic doctrine by such a weak, conjectural proof is far-fetched.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents eight points (وجوه) that are part of the first argument (الحجة الأولى) for the immateriality of the soul, specifically addressing an objection based on the weakening of intellectual faculties in old age. The author lists eight reasons why intellectual perception can remain strong despite bodily decline. First, the necessary capacity remains. Second, the intellect's organ is resistant to decay. Third, the old man's temperament may favor intellect. Fourth, weakness in old age reduces the interference of appetitive and irascible powers, allowing equilibrium. Fifth, accumulated premises and experience compensate for bodily decline. Sixth, repeated actions produce stable dispositions. Seventh, a prophetic saying shows that certain attributes like greed and hope persist despite bodily weakness. Eighth, even if the soul were a subtle body, bodily decline would not affect it. The author then concludes that although these points can be debated, the overall proof remains weak and conjectural, and such a weighty doctrine as the soul's immateriality cannot rest on such a fragile argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These eight points collectively provide a strong proof for the soul's immateriality.
Correction: The author explicitly states that these points make the proof weak and conjectural, and that proving immateriality requires stronger evidence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to the appetitive and irascible powers in old age that benefits intellectual perception?
A) They become stronger and aid the intellect.
B) They weaken, allowing the intellect to reach equilibrium.
C) They remain unchanged.
D) They combine with the intellect to form a single power.

Key Takeaway
The eight points defend intellectual continuity in old age, but their weakness shows that the soul's immateriality cannot be proven by such arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Imagine that you are an old person whose intellect remains sharp. Does that fact alone convince you that your soul is immaterial? If not, why do you think people are drawn to such arguments? What does your answer reveal about your own desire for certainty?

Eighth Objection: The Soul May Be a Luminous Body
Al-Razi raises an objection that the first proof does not establish the soul's simplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's objection that the soul could be a luminous, non-decaying celestial body, which challenges the proof that the soul is a simple substance.

Core Idea
Bodily weakness does not prove soul weakness if the soul is made of celestial matter.

Al-Razi continues his series of objections to the proof of the soul's simplicity. Here he argues that even if the body weakens, the soul may remain strong if it is composed of luminous celestial bodies that do not decay. This objection forces us to reconsider the link between bodily states and the soul's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Al-Razi begins with 'The eighth: This proof does not indicate...' This is a direct critique of a previous argument.
The previous argument tried to prove simplicity from bodily weakness.
2
Identify the assumption
Al-Razi asks us to suppose that the soul is a luminous, pure, celestial body different from earthly elements. Identify what properties such a body would have: it does not accept dissolution, withering, or change.
These are the exact phrases in the text: 'لا تقبل الانحلال والذبول والتبدل'.
3
Understand the logical consequence
If the soul is such a body, then when the physical body weakens, it does not force the soul to weaken. The conclusion that the soul is a simple substance does not follow from the premise of bodily weakness.
This is a classic objection from a materialistic (but non-elemental) conception of the soul.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection matters: it shows that the proof from bodily weakness is not sufficient to establish the soul's immaterial simplicity. The soul could be material but made of superior matter.
Later responses will need to address this by showing that even celestial bodies are still material and subject to change in some sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : إن هذه الحجة لا تدل على كون النفس جوهراً مجرداً ، لأن بتقدير أن تكون النفس عبارة عن أجسام مشرقة صافية سماوية مخالفة للطبائع العنصرية ، وأنها لا تقبل الانحلال والذبول والتبدل . فحينئذ لا يلزم من حصول الضعف والانحلال في البدن حصولهما في جوهر النفس .
The eighth: This proof does not indicate that the soul is a simple substance, because if it is assumed that the soul consists of luminous, pure, celestial bodies, different from elemental natures, and that they do not accept dissolution, withering, or change. Then, in that case, the occurrence of weakness and dissolution in the body does not necessitate their occurrence in the substance of the soul.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is an objection (the eighth point) to a previous argument attempting to prove the soul is a simple substance. The objector argues that the evidence of bodily weakness does not prove the soul's simplicity because the soul could be composed of luminous bodies that are not subject to decay. It does not follow that because the body weakens, the soul must also weaken. The argument assumes that the soul is not made of elemental matter but of celestial, non-decaying material. Thus the proof fails to establish the soul's non-material simplicity.

Misconception: One might think that if the body weakens, the soul necessarily weakens.
Correction: This passage clarifies that is not necessarily so if the soul is of a different nature, such as luminous celestial bodies immune to decay.

Checkpoint

A) The proof is valid and conclusive.
B) The soul could be composed of luminous bodies immune to decay.
C) The soul is definitely material.
D) The proof is based on false premises.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's objection shows that the proof from bodily weakness fails to establish the soul's simplicity because the soul might be a non-decaying luminous body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objection suggests that the soul might be of a different nature than the body, not subject to decay. If you truly believed your soul is indestructible, how would that change the way you face your own mortality and aging? Are you living as if your soul is beyond decay, or are you consumed by the decay of your body?
?
Al-Razi points out that the evidence from bodily weakness does not prove the soul's simplicity. Do you ever demand absolute proofs for spiritual matters? Could your insistence on empirical evidence be a hindrance to faith? What would it mean for you to accept uncertainty about the nature of the soul while still living a life of devotion?

The First Proof: Self is Not the Body
Author presents first proof for the soul's distinct existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the soul's existence as distinct from the body, including its syllogistic structure and the three arguments that support the premise of bodily change.

Core Idea
The self is not the body because the body changes but the 'I' does not.

This lesson introduces the first of several proofs that the soul is a distinct entity from the body. The author presents a syllogism and then offers three compelling observations from daily life and physiology that confirm the body is in constant flux. As you read, notice how each argument builds on the premise that change is inherent to the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
The passage is syllogistic: premises followed by three sub-arguments.
2
Identify the syllogism
Find the major premise (the body changes), the minor premise (the 'I' does not change), and the conclusion (the body is not the 'I').
The conclusion is stated at the beginning.
3
Study the first argument
Examine the first supporting argument: the body's hot, moist composition leads to constant dissolution and need for nourishment.
This is a physiological argument.
4
Study the second argument
Consider the growth from infant to adult, showing parts must change to accommodate growth.
This is an observable developmental change.
5
Study the third argument
Reflect on weight fluctuations (fat/thin/fat) as clear evidence of change in bodily parts.
This argument uses everyday experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس في الدلائل التقوية المعتبرة في اثبات النفس الحجة الأولى : إن هذا الجسد أجزاؤه واقعة في التبدل . والمشار إليه لكل أحد ، بقوله : ( أنا ) : غير واقع في التبدل ، ينتج : أن هذا الجسد ليس هو الشيء المشار إليه بقوله : ( أنا ) . أما المقدمة الأولى : فالدليل على صحتها وجوه : الأول : إن جسد الإنسان جوهر حار رطب . والحرارة إذا عملت في الرطوبة ، أصعدت عنها البخارات ، وذلك يوجب انحلال بدن الإنسان ولهذا السبب احتاج هذا البدن إلى الغذاء ، ليقوم الغذاء بدل ما تحلل . فثبت : أن التحلل والذوبان : دائم الحصول في بدن الإنسان الثاني : إن بدن الإنسان حال ما كان طفلاً ، كان شيئاً صغيراً ، وحال صيرورته شاباً ، يصير جسماً عظيماً . وذلك يوجب كون أجزائه في التبدل الثالث : إن الإنسان قد يكون سميناً ، ثم يصير هزيلاً ، ثم يعود سميناً . فالأجزاء قد تبدلت لا محالة بالزيادة والنقصان .
Chapter Six: On the strong proofs considered in establishing the soul. The first proof: This body, its parts are in a state of change. And that which is pointed to by everyone with his saying 'I' is not in a state of change. It results that this body is not the thing pointed to by the saying 'I'. As for the first premise, the evidence for its correctness has aspects: First: The human body is a hot, moist substance. Heat, when it acts upon moisture, raises vapors from it, and that necessitates the dissolution of the human body. For this reason this body needs nourishment, so that nourishment replaces what has dissolved. Thus it is established that dissolution and melting are constantly occurring in the human body. Second: The human body, when it was an infant, was something small, and when it becomes a youth, it becomes a large body. That necessitates that its parts are in change. Third: A person may be fat, then becomes thin, then returns to fat. So the parts have changed inevitably by increase and decrease.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the existence of the soul (nafs) as distinct from the body. It is a syllogistic argument. The author states the major premise: the body is in constant change (tabaddul). The minor premise: that which each person refers to as 'I' (ana) is not subject to change. The conclusion: therefore, the body is not identical to the self (the 'I'). The author then provides three supporting arguments (wujuh) for the first premise (that the body is in change). The first argument is based on the physical composition of the body as hot and moist; heat acting on moisture causes vapors and dissolution, requiring food to replace lost parts, proving constant change. The second argument observes the change from infant to adult, showing that the body's parts must change for growth. The third argument notes observable changes in weight, from fat to thin and back, again demonstrating change in the body's parts. These arguments reinforce the premise that the body is not static, thereby strengthening the overall proof that the self is not the body.

Key Terms
النفس
الجسد
التبدل
الحرارة
الرطوبة
التحلل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the self is identical to the body because they are closely associated.
Correction: This argument shows that their differing properties (change vs. non-change) prove they are distinct.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the first proof for the soul?
A) The body is constantly changing.
B) The self is not the body.
C) The soul is a hot, moist substance.
D) The body needs nourishment.

Key Takeaway
The body's constant change, evident in growth, nutrition, and weight fluctuation, proves that it cannot be the unchanging 'I' that each person refers to as 'self'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look in the mirror and notice signs of aging, who is the 'you' that observes that change? Has that observer ever aged or changed? What does this passage demand you to admit about yourself?
?
Recall a time when your body went through a major change—weight loss, injury, or growth. Did the feeling of being 'you' ever waver? If the body is constantly replaced, why do you still feel like the same person? Are you ready to accept that the 'I' is not the body, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
If your body were completely replaced over time (like the Ship of Theseus), would you still be the same person? This passage says yes, because the 'I' does not change. How does that change the way you value your physical appearance or health? Will you now prioritize your soul over your body?

Fourth Proof: Body's Constant Dissolution
Al-Razi presents a fourth empirical proof of the body's constant dissolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's empirical proof that the body is not permanent but constantly undergoes dissolution.

Core Idea
The body appears stable, but it is continuously losing parts.

Al-Razi now turns to a common observable phenomenon to demonstrate the body's impermanence. He uses the example of bathing and the reappearance of dirt to argue that the body's parts are constantly changing. This proof relies on everyday experience, making it accessible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation to grasp the gist of the proof.
2
Identify the empirical observation
Note the bathhouse example: after thorough cleaning, new dirt appears a week later. This is the core evidence.
3
Understand the reasoning
Al-Razi explains that the new dirt comes from parts that were alive and attached but have since died and separated.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that all parts of the body are subject to change, dissolution, and decomposition.
This supports the idea that the body is not a permanent substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنا نشاهد كون هذا الجسد واقعاً في الذوبان بسبب انفصال الرطوبات الكثيرة منه، وبسبب انفصال العرق والوسخ. فإن الإنسان إذا دخل الحمام، وبالغ في تنظيف الجلد من الأوساخ، حتى صار ظاهر الجلد بحيث لو أعيد إليه الحك والدلك يخرج منه الدم. ثم إذا خرج من الحمام وبقي أسبوعاً ثم عاد ودخل الحمام مرة ثانية، فإنه يجد في الجلد شيئاً كثيراً من الأوساخ. وما ذاك إلا لأن الأجزاء التي كانت ملتصقة بالبدن في المرة الأولى التصاقاً طبيعياً وكانت حية، فإنها في هذه المرة الثانية يبست وانفصلت عن البدن وزالت الحياة عنها. وهذه الاعتبارات بأسرها دالة على أن جميع أجزاء هذا الجسد واقعة في التبدل والذوبان والانحلال.
Fourth: We observe that this body is in a state of dissolution due to the separation of many moistures from it, and due to the separation of sweat and dirt. For if a person enters the bathhouse and thoroughly cleanses the skin of dirt until the surface of the skin becomes such that if rubbing and scraping were applied to it, blood would come out. Then if he leaves the bathhouse and stays for a week, then returns and enters the bathhouse a second time, he finds on the skin a great amount of dirt. This is only because the parts that were attached to the body in the first instance with a natural attachment and were alive, have in this second instance dried up, separated from the body, and life has left them. All these considerations indicate that all parts of this body are subject to change, dissolution, and decomposition.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof that the body is not permanent. It uses empirical observation: the body constantly loses matter through sweat, dirt, and dead skin cells. The example of cleaning the skin in a bathhouse shows that after thorough cleaning, new dirt appears after a week, indicating that old skin cells die and fall off. This demonstrates that the body's parts are constantly changing and dissolving. The conclusion is that the body is not a permanent entity.

الانحلال

الأجزاء
